CHINA
ZHEJIANG PROVINCIAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
Loan and Proiect Summary
Borrowers:            People's Republic of China
Beneficiary:          Zhejiang Province
Amount:                $220 million equivalent
Terms:                 20 years, including 5 years of grace, at the Bank's
standard variable interest rate
Onlendina Terms:      20 years, including 5 years of grace, at 6 percent per
annum with 0.75 percent per annum commitment fee on the
unwithdrawn balance. Zhejiang Province would bear the
foreign exchange risk.
Project Obiectives:   The project has the following specific objectives:
(a) to relieve traffic congestion which is restraining
economic growth in the Hang7hou-Ningbo Highway corridor,
and provide a high standard road transport link in sup-
port of agricultural and industrial development; (b) to
provide support for a Road Development Program prima:ily
in the economically less developed southern regions of
the Province; (c) to further develop institutional capa-
bilities for transport planning and determination of
investment priorities; and (d) to promote the transfer
of highway construction and maintenance technology
through training and provision of modern equipment.
Project Description:  The project includes:  (a) construction of a 138 km
section of the four-lane divided, access controlled
Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway on a new alignment; (b) con-
struction and improvement of about 33 sections of
provincial and county roads (970 km); (c) consultant
services for construction aupervision of the Hangzhou-
Ningbo Highway; (d) staff training; and (e) purchase of
equipment for road laboratories, environmental protec-
tion, road research, a road data bank, a pavement man-
agement system, road maintenance and highway operation.
Proiect Benefits:      Primary benefits from the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway will
be in the form of road user cost savings resulting from
improved vehicle operating costs and time savings due to
reduced length of the new highway. Other benefits will
stem from reduced congestion on the existing road and
accelerated industrial and agricultural growth based on
improved transport facilities. Benefits from the Road



Development Pro&ram will come from the provision of
improved all-weather access for motorized vehicles to
presently isolated rural areas. Additional benefits
will accrue from maintenance cost savings on the
improved provincial/county road network.
Risks:                Because of the difficult subsoil conditions along the
alignment of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway, the major risk
is delay in the completion of the highway and the qual-
ity of construction. Since the implementing authority
for the highway is a first-time executing agency for a
Bank project, there is also a risk in implementing the
project in a timely manner. However, the preparation of
the project was assisted by the Bank's experience in
previous Chinese highway projects, and by the more
active involvement of the Ministry of Communications;
this enhanced role of the Ministry is expected to con-
tinue into fajtiure projects. To minimize the risks, the
project was designed wv-h  t     z.e -stance of foreign
experts, prequalification of contractors was made par-
ticularly stringent, and supervision arrangements were
carefully formulated to ensure regular monitoring and
effective quality control.

CHINA
ZHEJIANG PROVINCIAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
I. THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
A. Introduction
1.1      The transport system in China is characterized by intensive freight
traffic in relation to economic output, heavily suppressed demand, high utili-
zation of assets, a strong emphasis on railways, and distortions in tariffs
and costs. Freight traffic intensity in China, measured in ton-kilometers per
dollar of GNP, is even higher than in the (former) Soviet Union, and is much
higher than in India, Brazil or the United States (Table 1.1). The major rea-
sons behind China's high freight traffic intensity are a relatively large
heavy industry sector, reliance on coal as the major source of energy, and a
large volume of other bulk products.  In adlition, energy efficiency in China
is relatively low, and there is still little preprocessing of raw materials
before shipping. On the other hand, the density of China's transport network,
measured both on the basis of geographic area and population, is among the
lowest in the world (Figure 1 and Table 1.2).
Figure 1: INTERNATIONAL COMPARISON OF LAND TRANSPORT NETWORKS
Inwnauonal Comparisons of Railway Networks    nmaidoonal Compaisws of Road Neworks

B. Traffic
1.2      Preight Traffic.  The economic reforms introduced since 1979 have
provided the impetus for a large upsurge in both freight and passenger traf-
fic, severely taxing the transport system. Freight traffic has grown at about
9 percent per annum; port freight traffic also has grown at about 9 percent
per annum; and intercity highway freight traffic has grown at about 18 percent
per annum--consistently higher than the growth of gross domestic production
(Table 1.3). These rates would have been even higher were it not for the
capacity constraints which are evident in all modes. Many power plants and
factories have fallen short of their production goals because the transport
s'stem has not been able to deliver the needed raw materials in time. Coal
production in Shanxi province, the largest coal producer, continues to be



- 2 -
hampered by inadequate rail capacity. Rural areas throughout the country are
short of transport services, both for crops a;; for local light industry.  The
capacity of navigable waterways has declined due to lack of investment in
upgrading and maintaining navigation channels. At the ports, lack of modern
facilities limits the handling in bulk of commodities such as fertilizers,
cement, and grain.
1.3       PassenAer Traffic.  The transport system is further taxed by the
marked growth il the demand for passenger travel in the last decade. Since
the economic reforms of 1979, passenger traffic has grown at nearly 12 percent
per annum, illustrating the positive relationship '-tween growth in income and
growth in demand for travel (Table 1.4).  Nonetb    J, passenger travel per
capita in China remains very low, in part because if severe capacity con-
straints. Civil aviation traffic, despite growing much faster than any other
mode, is still very limited, and most long-distance journeys are made by raLl.
1.4       TransDort Demand and Modal Split Trends.  Although the railway's
share of traffic has declined over the last 1"-20 years and the trend is
likely to continue as the share of road traffic grows, unlike in other coun-
tries, rail transport still dominates freight and passenger traffic in China.
In 1990, 58 percent of freight (Table 1.3) and 46 percent of passenger traffic
(Table 1.4) were moved by rail, and the rest was moved by road, inland water-
way, coastal shipping, pipeline, and civil aviation. The continued preemi-
nence of the railway is in part due to the structure of the Chinese economy,
wherein heavy industry is prominent and coal is the dominant energy source.
Coal and the large quantities of other bulk inputs associated with heavy
industry are well-suited for railway transport and constitute the majority of
railway traffic ia China.  In the case of passenger transport, it is due
partly to the virtual absence of private automobiles, and partly to the
limited use of aviation. Civil aviation's share was only 3.1 percent for
passenger traffic and 0.04 percent for freight traffic.
1.5      As a result of the slowdown in economic activities in 1989/1990,
transport demand, particularly of passengers, dropped significantly, and tem-
porarily alleviated the congestion in transport facilities.  Preliminary fig-
ures indicate that resumption of high economic growth in 1991 has resulted in
a corresponding surge in transport demand.

C. II3veD nt
1.6       The Government of China has repeatedly pronounced transport bottle-
necks to be one of the moat severe constraints to economic growth. However,
transport investment level. have not kept pace with the increased demands.
Thus, vhile annual investments in the transport sector measured in current
prices surged approximately three times during the period of the fifth, sixth
and seventh five-year plans (1976-90), the share of total public investment
dedicated to transport remained quite steady over this period, averaging about
13 percent (Table 1.5). Further, investments in transport infrastructure
represented about 1.4 percent of GNP during 1980-90, compared to 2-3 percent
for countries such as South Korea and Brazil. Transport investments as a per-
centage of GNP have declined from a high of 2.4 percent during the Fourth
Five-Year Plan (1971-75), to 1.3 percent during the Seventh Five-Year Plan
(1986-90) (7FYP), despite the absolute increase in transport investments dur-
ing the period (Table 1.6). In the future, a substantial expansion of invest-
ments will be needed if the transport-related constraints to economic growth
are to be removed.
D. Transport Issues and Objectives
1.7       The key issues in the transport sector ares
(a) chronic shortages in the supply of transport services due to under-
investment, resulting in severe economic losses due to unmet demand;
(b) excess demands generated by underpricing of transport services and
transport-intensive commodities (particularly coal);
(c) uneven development of modes (i.e., excessive reliance on rail, rela-
tive neglect of other modes, and the absence of modern, integrated
intermodal services);
(d) lack of modern technology within each mode (obsolescent vehicles,
infrastructure, and operating systems), resulting in high operating
costs and poor services;
(e) shortages of human resource skills in specific key areas, particu-
larly in multimodal planning and policy analysis, economic analysis,
and user-centered logistics management; and
(f) structural weaknesses in highway construction capabilities related
to project and company management, quality control and procurement
procedures.
1.8       Achieving a better balance between demand and supply is vital
because chronic shortages of transport services have been increasingly con-
straining the growth of the Chinese economy. The effects have been seen in
the past in electric power shortages due to lack of coal transport capacity,
but transport constraints pose economic problems well beyond their effects on
the energy sector. The poor quality of transport services resulting from
shortages of often outmoded equipment and substandard, often congested infra-
structure, imposes high costs and impedes the integration of markets, which is



-4-
one of the major sources of economic efficiency in modern societies. The
ability of China to compete in world export markets is also reduced, particu-
larly for light manufacturing and high-value agricultural exports, which
require efficienr, flexible, fast door-to-door services.
1.9       Despite recent adjustments, pricing distortions are still a major
problem. Cost analyses conducted during a recent study in Guangdong Prov-
ince 1/ suggest that, despite substantial tariff increases in 1989, nation-
wide long-distance coal transport, for example, is underpriced by as much as
40 percent relative to long-run incremental costs, thus generating uneconomic
excess demands which clog the transport system. Distortions in the opposite
direction occur in highways and aviation, where tariffs may exceed long-run
incremental costs, thus distorting the choice of modes. So far, enterprises
have lacked appropriate incentives to set tariffs to reflect costs as they
have had only limited financial autoi.omy, and competition among transport
modes has been minimal due to regulatory restrictions and the congested condi-
tion of the system.
1.10      China has so far failed to acquire the full spectrum of modern
transport technology. It still relies on the railway to handle much traffic
that could be better served by modern highway vehicles and by the airplane.
Both these modes are modernizing and are steadily increasing their share of
total traffic, in highways for both freight and passengers, and in aviation
especially for the movement of people. There is also some railway traffic
which could be more economically served by coastal shipping. The Ministry of
Railways in recent years has significantly increased tariffs for short-haul
commodities, so as to encourage diversion of this traffic to other modes and
to concentrate on long-haul bulk commodities, where its comparative advantage
lies.
1.11      Over the long run, China's choi 3 of which modes to expand and by
how much should be based on an understanding of the differences in costs and
service characteristics of the different modes, on the one hand, and prospec-
tive changes in the structure and level of transport demand, on the other. At
the present time, there is a need for China to train planners and policy ana-
lysts in the analytical tools necessary to evaluate those choices and to iden-
tify the instruments required to effect the necessary changes.
1.12      Transport Sector Reforms.  The Government has already taken steps to
respond to some of these challenges. Notably, it has:
(a)  made major adjustments in transport tariffs and is conducting cost-
ing studies that will provide the basis for further adjustments. In
addition, it has allowed local authorities more autonomy to plan,
finance, and implement projects which will expand transport capa-
city;
1/   Guangdong Province Comprehensive Transport Study, Transportation Divi-
sion, Country Department III, Asia Regional Office, World Bank, July 7,
1991.



-5-
(b) allowed private individuals and commercially oriented collective and
township enterprises to provide passenger and freight transport
services on roads and inland waterways and has encouraged provincial
authorities to develop local railway systems;
(c) carried out major structural reforms in the organization and manage-
ment of civil aviation and in the ports, leading to increased finan-
cial autonomy and accountability, less central government controls
and paving the way for introduction of competitive services;            a
(d) reduced operating and capital subsidies for major infrastructure
services, through higher cost recovery and creation of user-funded
capital construction funds;
(e) encouraged introduction of more modern technology in the transport
system primarily by improving rolling stock technology in the rail-
way subsector; enhancing container and bulk handling capacity in the
port subsector; improving road pavement design and geometric stan-
dards for the expansion of motorization in the highway subsector;
and increasing the use of computerized data processing and planning
techniques in operations in all modes.
E. Bank Group Participation in Transport Sector
1.13      The Bank Group has supported the initiatives of the Government in
the transport sector through projects, sector studies, technical assistance,
and training. Through December 1991, the Bank Group had loaned about $2.5
billion to China under 20 projects for the transport sector and presently is
preparing several new transport sector projects.
1.14      The railway subsector has received $1.37 billion in Bank Group fund-
ing under six projects (Loan 2394-CHA; Loan 2540-CHA; Loan 2678-CHA/Credit
1680-CiA; Loan 2968-CHA; Loan 3060-CiA/Credit 2014-CHA and Loan 3406-CHA).
These projects have assisted with the expansion of capacity on key routes and
the manufacture of electric locomotives, passenger coaches, and signaling
equipment; one project is assisting construction of a line in Inr,er Mongolia.
The most recent project is assisting with rail technology modernization;
development of systemwide plans, planning tools and costing studies; and add-
ing capacity where it is urgently needed. The audit of the first Railway
Project noted that physical components were well executed and that macro-
policy adjustments, especially in the area of tariff reforms, and investment
prioritization were likely to have the highest impact; these conclusions have
been considered in designing the railway project currently under preparation.
1.15      The highway subsector has received $722.2 million in Bank Group
financing under seven projects (Loan 2539-CHA/Credit 1594-CRA; Loan 2811-CHA/
Credit 1792-CiA; Loan 2951-CRA/Credit 1917-CHA; Loan 2952-CHA; Credit 1984-
CHA; Loan 3073-CiA/Credit 2025-CiA; Loan 3316-CHA/Credit 2226-CHA). These
projects provide for the construction of high-quality national and provincial
roads totaling about 1,276 km, and the expansion and improvement of some
3,250 km of rural (lower class provincial, county, village) roads and major
bridges. When completed, some of these roads will help to provide vital miss-
ing links in the national highway network; others will help to ease severe



- 6 -
congestion in parta of the network or provide access to remote areas. The
projects are also designed to provide foreign technical assistance and train-
ing of Chinese personnel in areas such as supervision and quality control of
road construction, design, and planning. Finally, the projects support stud-
ies on key issues of highway development such as road safety, pavement manage-
ment, user charges, and methods of financing the expansion, improvement, and
rehabilitation of the road network. The first Highway Project (Loan 2539-CHA,
Credit 1594-CHA) is completed5 a PCR is scheduled to be issued in FY92.
1.16      The ports subsector has received $495 million of Bank Group finan-
cing under six projects (Loans 2207-CHA, 2689-CHA, 2907-CHA, 2877-CHA, 3006-
CHA, and 3007-CHA). Host of the loan funds are being used in Shanghai,
Tianjin, Dalian, Huangpu, Ningbo, and Xiamen for construction of terminals,
jetties, and berths for handling bulk commodities, general cargo, and contain-
ers, as well as the dredging of navigation channels. Funds also have been
used for training, technical assistance, and various studies such as port
planning, operations management, and containerization. The audit report of
the first port project found the project successfully completed, on time and
below cost, but recommended developing long-term strategic port investment
planning and improving prequalification procedures; recommendations on the
former point have been incorporated in the preparation of the forthcoming port
project in Shanghai, and recommendations on the latter have been taken into
account in the discussion of procurement procedures in transport projects
under preparation.
1.17      The first urban transport project (Credit 2296-CHA, $60 million),
approved in September 1991, is intended to be the first stage of a long-term
program to upgrade and develop urban infrastructure in Shanghai. The project
will finance capacity increases on key urban links and will assist the
Shanghai Metropolitan Government to improve planning, programming, financing,
and management of its urban transport system.
1 18      The dialogue between the Government and the Bank Group on transport
sector operations and strategy has been strengthened both through the project
preparation activities and project-financed studies mentioned above and
through other studies carried out in collaboration with Chinese authorities.
A provincial transport study has been conducted in Guangdong Province (para.
1.9) to support the design of a new multimodal transport plan in the province.
The Bank Group also conducted a survey of the national water transport system,
which has reaffirmed the need to further exploit the large potential of the
Chinese waterways. In preparation for future highway lending, the Bank
assisted in the screening of national highway improvement proposals, aimed to
better identify highway construction priorities. Chinese authorities and the
Bank Group are currently preparing or undertaking new transport studies which
will focus on long-term strategies for the country transport system. These
studies include a multiport optimization study to evaluate medium- to long-
term options for developing the deep-water seaport at Ningbo; a study of stra-
tegic issues and the role of ports and shipping in southern China; a multimo-
dal transport study to examine strategic options in developing the transport
system for the Yangtze Economic Zone (Shanghai Municipality together with the
provinces of Jiangou, Zhejiang and Anhui); a coal transport study to evaluate
strategies for alleviating coal delivery shortages through the year 2005; a
railway investment study to optimize railway investments within a system-wide



approach, and a railway costing study that will allow the enterprise to estab-
lish cost-based tariffs. In the highways subsector, the Bank and MOC are
discussing preparation of a number of atudies that will cover finance, manage-
ment and other policy issues in infrastructure and in road transport services
(para. 1.35).
1.19      These projects and studies are helping to spur the growth and mod-
ernization of the transport system. However, much more needs to be done to
bring about the changes required to effectively deal with the emerging issues.
In order to significantly alleviate the transport bottlenecks and rationalize
the transport system, the government would need, by the year 2000, to take the
following measures:
(a) increase the total level of transport investment in relation to GNP,
to expand capacity to meet the new traffic demands and to clear up
the backlogs caused by past underinvestment;
(b) realize the most economical balance of investments among the major
freight transport modes of railway, highway, and water;
(c) realign further the prices of freight transport services and those
of the major commodities, especially coal, to bring them closer to
their true costs;
(d) increase further the productivity of existing transport assets
through technological modernization and management reforms; and
(e) improve multimodal investment decisions, both across and within
modes, through the use of analysis and planning tools that more
reliably determine the optimum economic priorities of transport
investments under budget constraints.
1.20      In the past, the Bank has supported the government in implementing
the above measures mainly by focusing on expanding traffic capacity and
improving operational efficiency. While such support will remain, the Bank
will broaden its strategy and will assist the government in the design and
implementation of institutional and sectoral reforms in other areas, including
investment planning, pricing and financing, services' deregulation, opera-
tional management, and transfer of advanced transport-related technology
through training and provision of up-to-date equipment.
F. The Hizhwav Subsector
The National Hiahwav Network and its Utilization
1.21      The hiahway network totaled about 1,028,000 km in 1990 and relative
to either population or geographic area, ranked among the smallest in the
world (page 1, Figure 1). About 30 percent of China's villages have no access
to roads suitable for motor vehicles. Investment in the highway subsector has
been rather modest, at 21 percent of total investment in the transport sector
during the 7FYP. Since the early 1980s, the highway system has borne an
increasing share of traffic. The network is, for the purposes of design and
technical specifications, classified by the Ministry of Communications (MOC)



- 8 -
as Expressway, and Classes 1, 2, 3 and 4 for various traffic capacities in
different terrain. These classifications are shown in Table 1.7, which also
shows the geometric standards now being used. For administrative purposes,
roads are classified as national, provincial, county, village, and special
purpose.
1.22      The rapid growth of hiahvav traffic stems not only from tie coun-
try's rapid economic growth but also from other factors such as the uiational
policy of shifting short-haul rail transport to road transport and the expan-
sion of the service sector and rural industries. Furthermore, with the
increase in foreign trade, growth in transport demand will be concentrated in
transport corridors serving the main ports and their hinterlands and, with the
containerization of the higher value commodities, much of domestic movements
for this foreign trade is expected to go by road. The new economic activities
have generated traffic in excess of what the existing facilities can accommo-
date. Congestion on roads resulting from the mix of slow-moving nonmotorized
and motorized traffic is a serious problem. Road pavements, which are gener-
ally well maintained, are showing increasing levels of roughness.
1.23      The vehicle fleet totaled some 5.5 million trucks, buses and cars in
1990, with trucks comprising over 71 percent. Most ttrcks are of four-ton
capacity with outmoded designs and low fuel efficiency. Most of the trucks
and buses are gasoline powered. These vehicles were manufactured domestically
and, until recently, the production of trucks was barely sufficient to replace
the retired vehicles, let alone meet growing demand. It was not until the
early 1980s that a large number of modern vehicles was imported and joint
ventures with foreign manufacturers were started to manufacture trucks; pro-
duction has grown steadily to reach 647,000 units in 1988, but dropped consid-
erably in 1989 and 1990 due to the economic stagnation in those years. This
production is contributing to the creation of a new fleet, although the older
vehicles still dominate. Growth in the truck and bus fleet has been fairly
steady over the last 12 years, with particularly strong grovth in small pas-
senger vehicles.
1.24      The tractor and motorcycle fleets, at 4.6 million and 4.2 million,
rival the size of the truck and bus fleet. Both tractors and motorcycles
offer a relatively cheap and basic form of transport, but add seriously to
traffic congestion on rural roads (principally tractors) and urban streets
(mainly motorcycles), and are a major cause of road accidents. Growth in the
tractor knd motorcycle fi ets has increased sharply, with motorcycles showing
the strongest growth of all vehicle types (Table 1.8).
1.25      The trucking industry consists of rublicly owned trucks operated by
trucking companies affiliated with local (provincial or district) highway
authorities, and other trucks operated by factories, cooperative organizations
and, recently, private individuals. The relaxation of trucking regulations in
the early 1980s allowed own-account trucks to haul cargo for other factories
and cooperatives and thus promoted competition between public and own-account
trucks. In fact, nonpublic trucks have absorbed most of the growth in traffic
over the past few years (Table 1.9). However, trucking costs are extremely
high, stemting in part from technological factors (poor roads, outmoded
trucks, etc.) but also from the lack of professional common carriers, which is
essential to the development of modern and efficient trucking services. While



-9-
market forces are expected to play a more important role in shaping the indus-
try in the future, it is necessary to understand the current constraints to
such development and to identify the measures needed to allow the reshaping of
the industry to take place.
1.26      Road safety is a major issue in China's transport system.  A study
completed in 1987 under the first Highway Project found the accident rate to
be 100 deaths per 10,000 vehicles, one of the highest in the world and costing
the Chinese economy close to $1 billion annually. The study also identified
essential measures needed to be taken, including improved police accident
reporting; introduction of computer-based accident analysis; identification of
accident causes and dangerous road spots; preparation of guidelines for road
safety design; improved administrative procedures; improved manpower training;
and preparation of a road safety action plan. Comprehensive treatment of the
road safety problem has been hampered by the transfer, early in 1988, of
responsibility for highway safety matters from MOC and the Provincial Trans-
port Departments (PTDs) to the Ministry of Public Security and its provincial
units. This decision has created administrative barriers which still have to
be overcome.
Hiazhwav Administration. Planni-na and Finance
1.27      The provincial governments, through the Highway Administration
Bureaus of the PTDs, are responsible for the administration of the highwav
network. Arrangements for administration vary among provinces; in the more
decentralized ones, the county and municipal governments perform some adminis-
trative and all of the operational functions relating to the highway network.
The central government's Ministry of Communications provides general policy
guidance and support to the PTDs through its nationwide policies and regula-
tions, specifying design and construction standards. HOC also provides spe-
cialized manpower for planning and design, and also construction equipment for
major provincial civil works, if needed. However, the PTDs are financially
independent of MOC and car-ry out all the road planning, construction, mainte-
nance, and administration of roads and waterways in the provinces. MOC is
more heavily involved only in some large projects which are considered of
national importance and for which MOC shares part of the financial burden.
1.28      While the provinces manage the country's highway network, MOC is
responsible for Plannina and investment strategies for the development of the
National Hiahwav System. A reclassification of the country's road network in
1981 led to the designation of key interprovincial links as National Highways.
More recently, MOC has been developing a long-term strategy for the systematic
upgrading of the national system in key corridors over a 30-year planning
horizon. The system, comprising twelve major routes, would connect all the
provincial capitals and cities with populations exceeding 500,000; route
selection would generally be based on the potential for maximizing industrial
production in the corridors selected for the initial, minimum-length network.
Construction of 25,000 km of high standard, access-controlled roads, is
expected to be completed by the year 2025. Priority for early construction is
being given to those routes already congested and hindering economic activity.
By the end of 1991, some 500 km of the system had been built, and a further
1,600 km is envisaged to be substantially completed by the end of the Eighth
Five-Year Plan (1991-95) (8FYP) in 1995. The provincial governments are



- 10 -
responsible for route location, engineering design, construction, operation
and maintenance of the national highway system.
1.29      The provinces finance hizhwav construction and maintenance through
the revenues generated by road maintenance fees, levied on the revenue of
freight and passenger transport companies and on the capacity of own-account
vehicles (para. 2.13). The MOC receives the revenues from a levy on vehicle
sales and uses these funds, which reach about Y 2 billion/year, to contribute
financing for: (a) the roads included in the national highway network, pro-
viding a higher share of financing for the poorer, hinterland provinces and a
lower share for the richer, coastal provinces; and (b) construction or reha-
bilitation of rural roads located in poverty-stricken areas. According to a
recent study by MOC, if existing sources for funding both national and provin-
cial roads are not improved, by the year 2000 they will provide 30 percent
less than is needed to develop the highway system in line with the growth in
demand. The issue is especially critical in the coastal provinces with the
fastest economic growth, where the road systems are experiercing the highest
levels of congestion and deterioration.
1.30      Over recent years, MOC has embarked on two important activities
designed to improve highway planning and investment decisions:
(a)  a computerized Road Data Bank (RDB), intended eventually to be
introduced in each provincial transport department. The road data
bank would contain all important road information at the national,
provincial and county level, providing the basis for improving the
economic rationale for decisions on expansion, rehabilitation,
strengthening and maintenance of the road networks. MOC's Highway
Planning and Design Institute (HPDI) has prepared a standard format
for the data to be inputted in the data bank and is setting guide-
lines for the provinces so that a uniform and fully integrated
nationwide system can be established; and
(b)  a Pavement Management System (PMS). This system would allow for the
development of economically appropriate standards for pavement
design and maintenance, leading to improved decision-making and
timing of investments. Following research initially sponsored by
the State Planning Commission (SPC) and the Ministry of Science and
Technology in 1986, HOC has taken the lead role in implementing
pavement management systems in the provinces, and pilot systems are
being set up in Guangdong, Shandong and Hebei, among others.
1.31      In addition to its role in planning and financing, MOC serves an
important function as coordinator for the construction of priority sections of
the national highway network. In early 1990, HOC established a Technical
Coordinating Unit (TCU) to maintain close contact with the provinces executing
projects financed by the Bank and other external financiers. TCU's main focus
has been to provide guidance to the project executing agencies and to coordi-
nate staff training arId the development of RDB and PHS at the provincial
level.
1.32      In summary, despite government efforts to improve the highway system
and its utilization and administration, much more is needed to modernize the



- 11 -
system and allow it to effectively meet the increasing demands placed on road
transport, both in improving physical capacity and the management of the sec-
tor. The key areas on which the government should focus attention to institu-
tional reforms and training are:
(a) cost recovery and financing, to provide the resources needed to meet
the increasing costs of maintenance and operations and to encourage
an efficient use of the highway system;
(b) highway planning and investment strategies, to better identify
short- and long-term highway development priorities;
(c) the role of MOC, to make it more consistent with the rapid decen-
tralization taking place in the management and financing of highway
operations, and to strengthen its capacities to deal with its
expanded role;
(d) preparation of feasibility studies using modern analytical tech-
niques, and based on data and relationships reflecting actual condi-
tions on China's highways;
(e) the operations of the road user services industry, both for freight
and passengers, to reduce costs and improve quality of services,
through the promotion of competition, modern technology and better
management; and
(f) the road construction industry, to improve the management and appli-
cation of contract conditions, modernize equipment, and clarify
relationships among clients, contractors and supervisory bodies.
Bank's Experience and Strate&v in Hishwavs
1.33      Of the seven Bank-financed highway projects (para. 1.15), only the
first Highway Project has been completed. The project, which was designed to
construct and improve 7 segments of the national and 59 segments of the
provincial/county road networks in nine provinces, achieved its original
objectives and clearly demonstrated the benafits accruing from improved acces-
sibility to communities deprived of adequate transport infrastructure in eco-
nomically less developed regions. Economic activity in the area of influence
of the project roads surged, and income levels increased noticeably. Further-
more, the Bank's involvement facilitated the acceptance of competition in the
contracting for road works, even for smaller civil works contracts, in prefer-
ence to force account operations, and demonstrated the merits of supervision
of construction by specially assigned units.
1.34      Highways together with port projects constitute the Bank's first
major involvement with large civil works in China. As could be expected, the
introduction of international competitive bidding has been difticult, particu-
larly as each project involves a new implementing agency. Despite these dif-
ficulties, which have placed most civil works components in highway projects
behind appraisal schedule, much progress has been achieved and the concept of
competitive bidding is now generally accepted. These projects have brought to
the surface weaknesses in the local contracting industry and contract adminis-



- 12 -
tration by the executing agencies. These lessons are being used in the prepa-
ration of new projects, for example by strengthening the prequalification of
contractors to avoid selection of unqualified contractors, and by strengthen-
ing arrangements for construction supervision. The Bank's involvement has
assisted MOC and the PTDs to develop a greater awareness of the need for sys-
tematic planning through the developmezst of the RDB and PHS. The Bank has
also helped in the transfer of technology through the supply of equipment for
road research and road maintenance, and through broad-based training programs
covering highway technology and advanced management practices.
1.35      The Bank's strate&v for future hiahwav lending is to continue to
support institutional development and sectoral reforms aimed at modernizing
the highway system and its management. This support will continue both at the
central and provincial government levels. The Bank's dialogue with central
and provincial authorities as well as the insights gained during supervision
Qf the highway projects portfolio have helped to identify key issues in the
highway sector and devise ways to address them systematically. While some
institutional and sector issues will normally be addressed in every project,
other issues requiring more detailed studies will be addressed selectively in
individual provincial projects, with a view to formulating recommendations
that may also serve other provinces (para. 1.36). During the next three to
five years, the investment components of highway projects will continue to
focus on high priority links of the national highway system, and to support
the development of the provincial road networks. The Bank strategy also
envisages continuing close collaboration with the MOC, and assisting it,
through economic and sector studies and through lending operations, in adapt-
ing to the ovolving needs of the Chinese economy.
1.36      Specifically, the Bank's strategy for forthcoming highway projects
comprises addressing the following issues:
(a)  Issues to be addressed in all hiLhwav irolects
(i)  sound management of the existing highway network, including
adequate provision for rehabilitation and maintenance, priori-
tized according to economic analysis;
(ii) development of high standard, limited-access highways in spe-
cific corridors of proven high demand and inadequate existing
capacity;
(iii) satisfactory compliance with environmental and resettlement
requirements; and
(iv) promotion of road safety.
(b) Issues to be addressed in s2ecific provincial highwav Droiects
(i)  Identification of a structure for adequate road user charges,
in order to mobilize the necessary resources for road develop-
ment and equitably recover from users the costs of the highway
system. A study would be conducted under the proposed
Guangdong Provincial Highway Project.



- 13 -
(ii)  Promotion of adequate road services, including encouragement to
private, collective and township enterprises to *nter the busi-
ness as part of the reform of the transport sector. A study at
the provincial level would be carried out under the proposed
nighway project in Henan Province, and a complementary study of
trucking at the national level would be conducted under the
Bank's transport sector program.
(iii) A study would be conducted under the proposed highway project
in Fujian Province regarding the need to upgrade the capacity
and effectiveness of the highway construction industry. This
study would be followed by a pilot program under the project
for the provincial construction industry.
(iv)  Integration of road transport services with other modes, par-
ticularly in the field of containerization, and the establish-
ment of modern, integrated multimodal services. The selection
of a highway project addressing this is still to be determined.
II. ZHEJIANG PROVINCE TRANSPORT SECTOR
A. Situation and Economy
2.1       Zhejiang Province is located on the coast of China immediately south
of the Yangtze River Estuary. The total land area is some 100,000 square
kilometers, but 70 percent of this is mountainous and a further 10 percent is
covered by lakes. The plain areas are concentrated in the northeast of the
Province; this region also contains most of the economic activity (Table 2.1).
About half the economic activity of the Province is concentrated in the 150
kilometer corridor linking the cities of Hangzhou and Shaoxing with the port
city of Ningbo. In 1990, Zhejiang ranked second in the nation in terms of
Gross Output Value of Agriculture and Industry (GOVAI). The Province as a
whole enjoyed a per capita GOVAI some 50 percent higher than the national
average, with the Hangzhou-Shaoxing-Ningbo corridor some 150 percent higher.
B. Transport Network
2.2       Zhejiang has a well developed transport system with tl. density of
roads, railways and waterways well above the national average (Table 2.2); in
particular, the density of the road and waterway networks is exceeded only by
Jiangsu Province. Nearly 4,000 of the Province's 10,600 kilometers of naviga-
ble waterways have a depth greater than one meter. The Port of Ningbo is the
only natural deep-water port on China's mid-eastern seaboard and is currently
being expanded with assistance from the Bank. The Province has well-developed
coastal shipping services, including freight and passenger ferry services
across the Hangzhou Bay to Shanghai, and the Province is well served by both
domestic and international air service. The size of the vehicle fleet is
relatively low, at 3.5 vehicles per 1,000 population compared with the
national average of 4.8. This is possibly because other modes of transport
are relatively well developed in Zhejiang. The number of vehicles per kilome-



- 14 -
ter of the provincial road network is the same as the average for the nation,
i.e., 0.2 km per vehicle.
2.3       The highway system of the Province has been expanded by about
33 percent in the years 1981 to 1990, and at the same time substantially
upgraded (Table 2.3). In particular, the length of paved road has been
doubled to about 6,100 km, 20 percent of the provincial highway system.  The
Province still has no Class 1 highways, and only 1,643 km of Class 2 highways.
The administrative classification of the road system in 1990 was as follows:
National         1,841 km        62
Provincial       5,149 km       172
County          13,909 km       462
Villasge         8,899 km       302
Special            397 km        1%
Total           30.195 km      1002
C.  Traffic Growth and Characteristics
2.4       Traffic growth in the five years from 1984-90 was strong, exceeding
the national average growth rate for all the major modes except rail freight,
where capacity limitations probably restricted activity. Growth on the high-
way system was particularly rapid, with an atmual growth rate of over 35 per-
cent for freight and approaching 14 percent for passengers. These high growth
rates in terms of ton-kilometers and passenger-kilom#,ters are reflected in the
changes in traffic densities since 1984 (Table 2.4).
2.5       In 1983, about 42 percent of the main network carried nonmotorized
or agricultural traffic, with less than 200 motor vehicles per day; by 1990
this proportion of lightly trafficked roads in the network had shrunk to less
than 6 percent. At the same time, the length of road carrying over 2,000
motor vehicles per day increased by thirty times (Table 2.5). These changes
are confirmed by the traffic counts in the Hangzhou-Ningbo Corridor which show
similar growth trends (para. 4.10). The growth in highway traffic can be
attributed to two main factors: the general expansion of the economy, and the
diversion of shorter distance traffic from other modes due to the improved
highway network, growing vehicle fleet, and liberalization of trucking ser-
vices in the early 19809.
D. The Vehicle Float.
2.6       The vehicle fleet increased by nearly three times between 1984 and
1990, at an average rate of 21 percent per year (Table 2.6). Most of this
growth was seen in smaller vehicles--medium and small trucks, small passenger
vehicles, and motorcycles, averaging annual growths of 47 percent, 34 percent
and 51 percent, respectively--while larger trucks and passenger vehicles grew
at a more modest rate of 11 percent per year. The fluctuations in the number
of agricultural tractors reflect unreliable historical data and changes in
licensing procedures in 1987 rather than an underlying trend--the total number
probably was fairly stable during the period with little, if any, growth.



- 15 -
E. The Trucking Industr=
2.7       The trucking industry in Zhejiang Province has followed nationwide
trends, in that an increasing volume of freight is being carried by own-
account operators--enterprises, collectives and private individuals--while the
tonnage carried by the public transport companies affiliated to the Provincial
Transport Department has dropped, both in total tonnage and as a share of this
subsector (Table 2.7). However, the average haul length of public transport
companies has more than doubled in the period, reflecting a more appropriate
role as a longer distance hauler, leaving more local pick up and delivery
services to others. The liberalization of the Trucking Regulations in the
early 1980s has clearly spurred the development of highway freight services in
the Province, and this is placing pressure on the capacity of the highway
system to absorb this traffic.
P. Highway Administration
2.8       The Zhejiang Provincial Transport Department (ZPTD) controls all
highway and waterway transport undertakings in the Province (Chart 1). The
ZPTD reports directly to the Zhejiang Provincial People's Government (ZPG) and
acts as an administrative arm of ZPG for planning, budgeting and financing of
transport in the Province. However, the ZPTD conforms to the general trans-
port policy and technical guidelines issued by HOC.
2.9       The Zhejiang Provincial Highway Administration Bureau (ZPHAB), under
the administration of ZPTD, is responsible for all highway matters and
receives professional and technical guidance from the Engineering Administra-
tion Department of MOC. With a headquarters staff of about 200, their main
function is the construction and maintenance of roads through the local admin-
istrations set up in the various municipalities, prefectures and counties of
the Province (Chart 2). The Transport Bureaus in each of the 11 municipali-
ties and prefectures undertake the construction and maintenance of the
national and provincial highways using experienced, full-time maintenance
units. Each of the 69 counties has responsibility under the municipality and
prefectures for the maintenance of the county roads. Some townships, enter-
prises and mines also take part in the maintenance of roads of their particu-
lar concern. The 11 municipalities and prefectures and the 69 county units
have a total staff of 2,270, of which technical personnel comprise about
26 percent.
2.10      The Zhejiang Provincial Shanghai-Hangzhou-Ningbo Expressway Project
Executive Commission (ZPEPEC) is a department level institution which reports
directly to ZPG and is exclusively responsible for the organization, direction
and coordination of the Shanghai-Hangzhou-Ningbo Expressway Project. It cur-
rently has a staff of about 200, of whom 120 are located in offices in the
cities along the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway (Chart 3).
G.  Plannnit. Budgetint and Financint
2.11      Plannina.  ZPTD has overall responsibility for planning investments
in and maintaining the provincial highway network. However, the highway
administration units at th,e prefecture, county, and village levels have con-
siderable autonomy in planning smaller road investments. These smaller



- 16 -
investments are subject to the approval of ZPTD, largely as a formality. Each
administrative group prepares investment plans for the highway network under
their jurisdiction, and submits them to the planning division of ZPTD by Sep-
tember of each year for the next year's plan, starting January let each year.
Before the end of November, ZPTD approves the next year's plan for each county
or local authority concerned. Similarly, ZPTD prepares the five-year road
development plan using inputs from lower level administrative groups revised
to reflect national or provincial development policy guidelines and objectives
for the plan period.
2.12      Budaeting. As is done in almost all sectors in China, ZPTD's plan-
ning division normally adopts a balanced budget policy. The proposed highway
investment is balanced against the revenue earmarked for highway development;
the amount of investment approved for each year is closely related to the
amount of road maintenance fees and other revenues expected for the same year.
This pattern has been followed in Zhejiang Province until 1987. Since then,
however, a special fund was established for reserving revenues for the con-
struction of high standard highways, such as the Hangzhou-Ningbo and Shanghai-
Ningbo Highways. In October of each year and before ZPTD approves the follow-
ing year's projects, the current year's planned investments ars reviewed
against actual investments and revenue receipts, and adjustments are made
where needed.
2.13      Financinft.  The road maintenance fee provides the main source of
furds to finance construction and maintenance of the highway sector. This fee
is collected by ZPTD from motor vehicles throughout the province. ZPTD deter-
mines the fee rates and, through the provincial government, reports the fee to
the State Council for approval before it becomes effective. The rates vary
according to the type and size of vehicles, as follows: (a) vehicles owned by
state transport companies are charged 15 percent of their gross revenue;
(b) vehicles owned by collective and private transport companies are charged
Y 120 per month per one payload ton, or Y 120 per month per 10 passenger
seats; and (c) passenger care are charged Y 70 per month. The rates reflect
increases effected in 1986 for state-owned transport companies and in 1988 for
collective and private transport companies. The maintenance fee provides the
source of income for all maintenance and overhead, and for a substantial por-
tion of upgrading costs. A maintenance fee surcharge is also collected at
highway check points at the rates of Y 25 per payload ton per month, 0.07 fen
surcharge per passenger-kilometer and 1 fen surcharge per ton-kilometer; these
funds accrue to a special fund for high standard highway construction. Cur-
rently, these funds are for the use of ZPEPEC.
2.14      The revenues from road maintenance fees are distributed as follows:
(a) 15 percent is remitted to the Ministry of Finance (MOF) as the Energy and
Transport Fund; (b) 10 percent is transferred to the State Budget Adjustment
Fund; (c) 2 percent is remitted to the Public Security Bureau (since 1987) for
highway patrol; and (d) 73 percent is retained by ZPTD for highway works.
Over the period, road maintenance expenditures increased at a rate of about
20 percent per annum. Of the total budget of Y 749.8 million in 1990, about
34 percent was expended on new highway construction and rehabilitation;
13 percent on road maintenance; and 2 percent on administration and overhead
costs. The remainder is used as reserve for the construction of the Shanghai-
Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway (Table 2.8).



- 17 -
2.15      In addition to the road maintenance fee, other resources for highway
works are obtained from: MOC, which contributes to new construction and major
upgrading of national and major provincial roads on a selective basis; and tile
provincial finance bureau, which provides budgetary resources for new provin-
cial road construction and emergency programs for flood damaged roads.
2.16      Total resources allocated for road uses in Zhejiang Province
amounted to 749.0 million in 1990, implying a real growth rate of 33 percent
per annum since 1985. In 1990, 63 percent of these funds originated from the
road maintenance fees; the rest came from MOC allocations and collection of
tolls. The MOC allocation, amounting to Y 42.0 million, was less than the
provincial contribution to MOF from the road maintenance fees collected from
highway users in Zhejiang Province, not counting fuel taxes.
2.17      Fuel prices in China are not subsidized.  Fuel taxes are charged to
road users but may not be allocated back to road works. These taxes are heav-
ily levied by MOF. They currently amount to 43 percent of the base price of
gasolines and 13 percent of the base price of diesel fuels. Total fuel
prices, including taxes, show that prices at the pump for planned fuel alloca-
tions are comparable to international market prices, while those charged in
the open market are well above international market prices and are increasing
at a rate comparable to the consumer price index (Table 2.9).
H. Engineerinf. Construction and Maintenance
2.18      Road and bridge projects are engineered by the planning and design
institutes at the provincial, prefecture, and county levels. The Zhejiang
Provincial Transport Design Institute (ZPTDI) is responsible for designing all
major road, bridge, and tunnel projects. In designing major highway projects,
ZPTDI has been assisted by several institutes of MOC:  (a) HPDI; (b) the First
and Second Survey and Design Institutes, located at Xi'an and Wuhan; and
(c) the Highway Scientific Research Institute (HSRI). In designing bridges
and tunnels, ZPTDI is also assisted by the First and Second Survey and Design
Institutes, and other specialized design institutes. HPDI is available to
assist in economic evaluation--a practice which is now becoming routine for
major road and bridge investments. HSRI provides advice on problems pertain-
ing to &eotechnical design and construction materials. Local design insti-
tutes are, in general, competent and have experienced personnel for carrying
out highway design tasks. However, the involvement of foreign consultants was
considered appropriate in the design of the Hangzhou-Ningbo highway, which is
the first high-standard highway to be designed and constructed in the Province
(para. 3.7). Foreign consultants would also assist ZPEPEC in supervising the
construction of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway (para. 3.16).
2.19      In the past, road and bridge construction normally was executed as a
force account operation by construction bureaus attached to the provincial,
prefecture, or county governments. Since SPC directed that all major civil
works be awarded after competitive bidding, a domestic contracting industry
has been growing rapidly. Many of the larger provincial construction bureaus
are being reorganized into financially independent contracting companies and
have been awarded contracts either individually or as partners in joint ven-
tures on previous Bank-financed highway projects.



- 18 -
2.20      The national and provincial roads are well maintained by the ZPTD.
However, due to the relatively low strength of pavements and the recent
increases in traffic, maintenance costs are rising rapidly. They presently
range between Y 5,000 and Y 6,000 per km per annum. Maintenance of the county
roads is well organized and is carried out effectively, but mainly through
labor-intensive operations. Under the project, the planning and execution of
both routine and periodic maintenance would be improved. Assistance would be
provided to proceed with the implementation of RDB and PMS as a basis for
preparing road investment and maintenance programs (paras. 3 18 and 3 19).
Basic maintenance equipment would also be provided (para. 3.21).
I. Highway Develonment
2.21      During the Seventh Five-Year Plan, the total transport sector
investment in the Province was Y 2.10 billion, about four times higher than
during the Sixth Five-Year Plan (1981-85). Of this amount Y 1.48 billion was
spent on the construction of new highways and rehabilitation of existing high-
ways; 4,884 km of new highways and to pave 2,119 km with high type (cement or
asphalt concrete) and sub-high type (gravel surface) pavements. The total
length of highway reached 30,195 km in the Province in 1990. During the
period of the 7FYP, 2,283 new bridges (of a total length of 62,400 m) and 33
tunnels (total length of 10,055 m) were built, and 2,820 km of highways were
built or rebuilt in economically depressed areas.
2.22      The recent acceleration of economic growth and the currently limited
number of high class highways has led the Province to increase allocations to
infrastructure for transport by water and roads, to about Y 6.47 billion dur-
ing the SFYP, or 232 percent higher than for the 7FYP. Investment in highways
is expected to amount to about Y 5.38 billion, or about 83 percent of the
total   The 8FYP aims at achieving the following goals in highway transport:
(a) Link all county towns to the main highway system by paved roads; it
is estimated that some 17 such towns remain to be connected, requir-
ing the construction of 575 km of roadsl
(b) Reconstruct and pave all roads carrying over 500 vehicles per day;
it is estimated that some 2,300 km of roads would require upgrading
for this reason; and
(c) Eliminate dangerous or substandard sections of existing roads at
such locations as: approaches to urban areas, narrow roads and
bridge bottlenecks, landslide areas, links to adjacent provinces,
at-grade railway crossings, and spots with a high rate of accidents.
2.23      By the end of the plan period, the total length of roads in the
Province is expected to increase to about 32,700 km (compared with nearly
30,195 km in 1990), including 200 km of expressways and 6,700 km of other
higher class roads. Besides the Hangzhou-Ningbo Expressway (145 km) included
in this project the Province plans to construct the 80 km Xiaoshan-Zhuji sec-
tion of the Hangzhou-Quzhou Road to motorway standards; continue rehabilita-
tion of Jinghua-Lishui Highway and Taizhou prefectural section of National
Road 104 to Class 2 standards; rehabilitate about 468 km of asphalt roads in
14 counties to Class 2/Class 3 standards; rehabilitate about 1,349 km of



- 19 -
National and Provincial roads carrying over 500 vehicles per day to Class
2/Class 3 standards; and build 7 new bridge., at Hengshan, Lingpu, Qingjiang,
Chingtian, Linghai, Huangyan and Bihu.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Formulation and Preparation
3.1       In the early 1980., the People's Republic of China (PRC) initiated a
comprehensive road development program which was implemented during 1986-90,
the period of the Seventh Five-Year Plan. The Eighth Five-Year Plan provides
for continuing this program at an accelerated rate, in recognition of the need
to expand and improve the national and provincial road networks to meet
increasing traffic demand in corridors with a high level of economic activity,
and to foster economic growth in the less developed regions of the country.
In 1989, the PRC requested the Bank's continued assistance for the subsector,
submitting to the Bank a list of highway proposals at the end of that year.
3.2       As a follow-up, a Bank mission, jointly with the SPC and HOC, vis-
ited several provinces during February and March 1990 to make a preliminary
assessment of nine highway proposals, identifying those highway projects eli-
gible for possible financing during the 8FYP. From this preselection process
it was concluded that the Zhejiang highway proposal was of high priority and
would be appropriate for Bank financing.
3.3       The proposed project was prepared under the direction of ZPTD with
the guidance of SPC and MOC. The Bank's project identification mission vis-
ited Zhejiang Province in March, 1989, with subsequent Bank missions working.
closely with SPC, HOC and the ZPTD to formulate the project scope. The main
components of the proposed project are the construction of a new highway
between Hangzhou and Ningbo, and a road development program for the upgrading
of provincial and county roads, primarily in the less developed southern
region of the Province (Map 1).
B. Proiect Obiectives and Rationale for Bank Involvement
3.4       The proposed project has the following specific objectives:
(a) to relieve congestion in the Hangzhou-Ningbo corridor by providing a
fast and reliable highway for long distance traffic serving the
industries located in the Hangzhou-Ningbo corridor and the Ningbo
Port area;
(b) to promote economic development, particularly in the southern region
of the Province, through the provision of road access to population
presently deprived of motorized vehicle access, and to improve the
provincial and county roads carrying substantial volumes of traffic;
(c)  to further develop institutional capabilities for transport planning
and investment prioritization; and



- 20 -
(d) to promote the transfer of highway construction and maintenance
technology through training and the provision of equipment.
3.5       The Bank Group's assistance strategy for China involves support for
implementing economic reforms, mo.'ernizing and rationalizing productive sec-
toro, and assisting with infrastructure development. Assistance with
transport investments specifically focuses on eliminating bottlenecks in the
system, improving rural infrastructure, and promoting modal interaction. The
proposed project is consistent with these assistance strategy objectives. The
main component of the project, construction of the Hangzhou-Nisngbo Highway,
was found to be a key priority link in the development of the national motor-
way network. It also complements earlier Bank assistance to Beilun Port near
Ningbo by improving access to the Port's newly constructed container terminal.
Although ZhejCang Province has a fundamentally sound approach to highway
development, it still lacks experience in the design and construction of mod-
ern highways, which are needed to eliminate bottlenecks in economically more
developed corridors. The proposed project will also help Zhejiang Province
adopt economic criteria and methodologies for investment decisions and intro-
duce technological improvements in the construction of rural roads. The
Bank's assistance in these areas is well placed since it has a comparative
advantage in supporting highway subsector development worldwide and in China
which is well recognized.
C. Proiect Scope
3.6       The scope of the project includes:
(a) construction of a 138 km section of the Hangzhou-Ningbo four-lane
divided, access-controlled highway, including service facilities, as
well as the supply and installation of electrical, electronic and
mechanical equipment for tolling, traffic monitoring, telecommunica-
tion and lighting;
(b) construction and improvement of about 33 road sections (970 km) of
the provincial/county road network and five bridges (2,000 m)
located primarily in the southern region of the Province;
(c) construction supervision of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway and the
provincial/county road construction and improvement components;
(d) purchase of equipment for:
(i) a central laboratory, environmental protection and research far
the Hangzhou-Ningbo highway;
(ii) operation and maintenance of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway;
(iii)  provincial/county road laboratories and survey units and for
the development and application of the Road Data Bank and Pave-
ment Management System; and
(iv) maintenance of the provincial/county road networks.



- 21 -
(e) staff training programs covering a broad spectrum of highway plan-
ning, design, construction and maintenance.
3.7       Construction of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Hiahwav.  The existing Hangzhou-
Ningbo road between Hangzhou and Shangyu, designated as National Route 104,
traverses populated and industrial areas, including the citias of Xiaoshan and
Shaoxing (Map 2), and extensive ribbon development, all of which has adversely
affected motorized vehicle speeds.  The section of the existing road between
Shangyu and Ningbo (designated as National Route 329) is more rural in charac-
ter with only one major city, Cixi. Although the existing road has been built
to Class 2 standards, it cannot accommodate the recent and expected future
increases in traffic beyond the year 1996. Road widening would not be feasi-
ble along most of its length because of the extensive side development. Even
if that could be done the traffic congestion through the cities and townships
would remain a serious impediment to normal traffic operations, and detrimen-
tal to economic growth.
3.8       The total length of the proposed highway is 145 km, of which 138 km,
and a 2.6 km access link to Ningbo City, would be constructed under the proj-
ect.  The construction of a 7 km section of the highway, including a major
bridge over the Qiantang River, was completed during 1988-91 and opened to
traffic in January 1992. High priority was accorded to the construction of
this section in order to relieve the severe bottleneck posed by the old and
narrow Qiantang bridge located just south of Hangzhou City. The works
involved have been financed jointly by the Province and the Hangzhou Munici-
pality.
3.9       A feasibility study, carried out by ZPTDI, analyzed four alternative
routes; the engineering design for the selected route was completed in mid-
January 1992. The design was undertaken by ZPTDI with the assistance of a
number of design institutes from HOC and other provinces, as well as the
Finnish Road Administration, in association with the Finnish Consulting Engi-
neering Ltd. The highway traverses geologically difficult terrain with exten-
sive soft, highly compressible, soils over two thirds of the length of the
alignment. An assessment of alternative solutions was undertaken with a 3 km
long section of highway embankment constructed for testing purposes to help
identify, through continued monitoring of settlements, the appropriate mea-
sures for mitigating the extent of consolidation.
3.10      The proposed highway would be constructed to expressway standards as
designated in the national geometric standards for highways (Table 1.7), with
connection to the existing road network to and from the new highway provided
at six interchanges. The highway project would also include 18 major bridges,
79 medium sized bridges, 98 small bridges, 31 overpasses, 14 road underpasses,
and 222 small underpasses, and would be operated as a toll facility (para.
4.19). In addition to civil works, the project also covers the cost of elec-
trical, electronic and mechanical installations required for toll collection,
traffic monitoring, telecommunications and lighting of major bridges and
interchanges. The final engineering design and technical specifications have
been reviewed by Bank staff with the assistance of the Finnish Road Adminis-
tration and Finnish Consulting Engineering Ltd. and were found to be satisfac-
tory.



- 22 -
3.11      The opening of the now highway would bring a significant increase in
traffic to some of the arterials both in the city of Hangzhou and, to a lesser
extent, Ningbo. The highway feasibility study highlighted the potential for
traffic congestion at the Hangzhou end of the highway, if timely actions were
not taken to increase the capacity of the main connecting artery (Genshan
Road) from the highway into the city. For this reason Genshan Road is pres-
ently being widened to accoimodate four traffic lanes vith provisions to seg-
regate motorized and nonmotorized traffic. Furthermore, works have also
started to widen some sections of the Inner Ring Road on the northern out-
skirts of the inner city. This is scheduled for completion by the end of
1995.
3.12      After the completion of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway, access to
Ningbo City would be through the interchange at Dazhujia and the junction at
Duantang, and the respective connecting access roads. Beilun Port and the
Beilun Development Zone would connect to the highway at Dazhujia through a
Class 2 access road which links up with Jianguan Highway and National Road
329. However, this Class 2 access road is expected to become congested during
the first decade of the next century. As a result, the construction of the
highway to Daqi, located at the southern part of the Beilun Development Zone,
has been tentatively scheduled to start around the year 2003, with the Right
of Way for this alignment already reserved (Map 2).
3 13      Road Development Program (RDP)   The experience gained from the
construction and improvement of secondary and tertiary roads under the first
Highway Project (para. 1.33) and under some of the ongoing provincial highway
projects confirmed the significance of the benefits accruing from this type of
investment, particularly in the lese developed regions in China. Using this
experience, the RDP has been formulated for the construction and improvement
of selected provincial and county roads and bridges located primarily in the
southern region of the Province. The RDP component represents about 20 per-
cent of the project's base cost. The lack of paved transportation infrastruc-
ture and poor condition of existing infrastructure have affected the standard
of living of the population. In particular, in six of the counties located in
this region the annual per capita income is Y 550, only half of the provincial
average of Y 1,100 (1990). Several county centers and numerous townships have
no motorized vehicular access, and goods in and out of these population cen-
ters are transported by animal-drawn carts and bicycles. Most of the roads in
the region are narrow and unpaved. In addition, some of the waterways are not
bridged, causing traffic to rely on ferry services which face problems during
both high- and low-water levels.
3.14      ZPHAB has, tentatively, identified 33 road and bridge subprojects
with a total length of 970 km for inclusion in the RDP, although subject to
further assessment subprojects may be deleted or added to the program within
the budgeted scope of the RDP. The subprojects so far identified fall into
the following four categories:
Group A: 11 subprojects, 465 km--requiring full-scale upgrading of geo-
metrics and pavement for the entire length of roads;



- 23 -
Group B:  5 subprojects, 201 km--bottleneck relief, involving widening
the pavement, realignment or construction of bypasses for town-
ships;
Group C:  4 subprojects, 11 km--building of bridges and bridge approaches
to replace ferry services or low standard bridges;
Group D:  13 subprojects, 293 km--construction of penetration roads to
provide motorized access to communities where no paved roads
exist.
3.15      The implementation of the RDP would be phased and subproject pack-
ages would be prepared and submitted to the Bank for approval at certain
intervals, primarily dictated by budgetary considerations for counterpart
funding. The criteria for subproject selection have been defined and include
the submission of an economic evaluation (with minimum EIRR of 12 percent),
engineering design, bidding documents, environmental assessment and/or envi-
ronmental action plans, and resettlement action plans for the Bank's clearance
(para. 6, Annex 1). The economic evaluation for the first package, comprising
seven subprojects, has already been completed as detailed in paras. 4.25-4.27
and in Annex 1 and was found to be satisfactory. Subject to submission of
engineering design, bidding documents and environmental and resettlement
action plans satisfactory to the Bank, the first package would qualify for
financing under the project. Assurances were obtained from ZPG at negotia-
tions that subnroiects to be included in the RDP would be selected in accord-
ance with criteria and procedures satisfactorv to the Bank and would be sub-
mitted to the Bank for approval. The approval of subnroiects would be a
condition of disbursement.
3.16      Supervision of Construction.  Although independent supervision of
construction contracts is gaining general acceptance in China, local staff
still have limited experience in supervising major highway works, particularly
those awarded under International Competitive Bidding (ICB). Based on the
experience gained from ongoing provincial highway projects, a team of Zhejiang
provincial personnel, local engineers, and technicians from other provincial
institutiors and foreign consultants would be established to supervise con-
struction of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway. The Joint Supervision Team (JST)
would be responsible for ensuring that construction works for the highway were
carried out in full compliance with specifications, and that strict quality
control were exercised on site. The organizational arrangements for supervis-
ing construction of the highway have been drawn up, and these arrangements and
terms of reference for the JST were agreed to during project appraisal. The
foreign supervising engineers would be integrated into the organization with
operational responsibilities. Furthermore, the foreign consultants would help
establish procedures at all levels of supervision, including on-site inspec-
tion and laboratory testing, and training of local staff both through formal
sessions and on-the-job. As this would include supervision of mitigating
measures identified under the environmental asasesment, staff training to
ensure compliance would also be included as necessary. About 174 man-months
of foreign consultant services are envisaged for supervision of construction
and related staff training.



- 24 -
3.17      Staff Training.  In addition to the training of local staff in
supervising construction, a series of training programs would be conducted in
transport/highway engineering and related areas. As with ongoing Bank-
financed provincial highway projects, the highway-related training program for
provincial staff would be coordinated by TCU of HOC in the interest of consis-
tency and economy. The training courses would be programmed in consultation
with the PTDs and the implementation administered by TCU. According to the
nature of the training, the courses would be provided either in China by local
institutions or abroad through training institutions of national or state road
authorities, and in some cases, by consultants. The main training courses
under preparation for provincial transport/highway personnel cover: transport
sector planning, highway planning, traffic survey and analysis, financial
management of highway projects, project planning and feasibility studies,
elements of project design (geometry, pavement, soft soil treatment, project
cost estimates), project implementation and supervision (contracting, quality
control), and highway operation and management systems (RDB, PMS, bridge main-
tenance system, equipment management system). The training areas of major
interest for Zhejiang provincial personnel were identified during project
preparation and are outlined in Table 3.1.
3.18      Road Data Bank.  A RDB to manage highway information has been devel-
oped in MOC for countrywide application. A standard format for the data has
been designed and a set of draft guidelines prepared to introduce the system
to the provinces in a phased manner. Initially, the Bank provided assistance
to MOC to help in the development of the RDB under the first Highway Project
(Loan 2539-CHA/Credit 1194-CA). This assistance has been followed up under
the ongoing Bank-financed highway projects in the provinces of Sichuan,
Shaanxi, Jiangxi, Shandong and Jiangsu. It has been used primarily to finance
the purchase of instruments, equipment, and computer hardware and software to
enable systematic collection and processing of input data for the system. The
same form of assistance is proposed under this project to meet the need for
equipment to implement the RDB in the Province (para. 3.21).
3.19      Pavement Manaaement System.  Increases in road traffic and in heav-
ier vehicles are taking their toll on the relatively light-structured pave-
ments in Zhejiang Province and the country as a whole. Although efforts have
been made to maintain the network over the years, most of these have been
focused on the short term and have had limited returns. Recognizing the need
for a systematic method of determining investment priorities, the MOC has
developed a Pavement Management System (PNS) which is being implemented in
several provinces. In Shandong, Shaanxi, Jiangxi, Sichuan and Jiangsu, imple-
mentation of the PMS has received assistance under Bank-financed highway proj-
ects. Recently, a leading group for PMS was set up in ZPHAB and a program has
been prepared for its operation. The project would provide assistance for the
PMS in Zhejiang by financing equipment and instruments for the pavement condi-
tion survey and data processing through computer applications (para. 3.21).
3.20      Equinment for the Hanazhou-Ninebo Highwav.  To set up and effi-
ciently operate a central laboratory in support of the construction supervi-
sion activitis-, there is a need for equipment and instruments, particularly
for testing soils and asphalt concrete. In the course of drawing up an Envi-
ronmental Action Plan (EAP), the equipment and instruments required to imple-
ment the environmental nrotection measures were identified. In view of the



- 25 -
severe subsoil conditions, the research activity related to the testing of
alternative subsoil treatments is intended to be continued, with the purchase
of relevant research eauipment and instruments. Equipment requirements for
the operation and maintenance of the highway have been identified; the major
items and estimated cost of equipment are shown in Tables 3.2 and 3.3.
3.21      Emuivment for the RDP and the Maintenance of Provincial/County
