Roads. ZPHAB, in consultation with the appraisal mission, identified the
following equipment requirements for acquisition under the project: (i) labo-
ratorv equipment and survey instruments for provincial and county road units;
(ii) specialized equipment, instruments and data processing hardware and soft-
ware for the aiDlication of RDB and PMS; and (iii) road maintenance eauipment
urgently required to replenish the equipment fleet of the provincial and
county road maintenance units. The major items and estimated cost of the
equipment are shown in Table 3.4.
D. Environmental Assessment
3.22       Hangzhou-Ningbo Hiahwav.  An Environmental Assessment (EA) for the
Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway was completed by the Zhejiang Provincial Environmental
Protection Research Institute (ZPEPRI) during 1989/90. It has been ascer-
tained that the highway will not affect ecologically sensitive areas and, if
appropriate actions are taken, it should not have any adverse impact on the
environment. Based on the EA's findings and subsequent to discussions between
the representatives of ZPG and Bank staff, an EAP has been prepared. This
Plan specifies the measures and organizational and monitoring arrangements
required for mitigating the potential adverse effects on the onvironment dur-
ing the construction and operation of the highway. The main measures relate
to social disruption, air quality, noise control, water management and control
of traffic, operation of quarries, and waste disposal. The engineering design
and technical specifications for the highway incorporate the mitigating mea-
sures required, and appropriate arrangements have been made to ensure their
application during construction of the highway. The RAP was reviewed during
project appraisal and was found to be satisfactory. A summary of the EA and
EAP is presented in Annex 2 and copies of the EA and RAP reports are available
in the Project File. Assurances were obtained from ZPG at negotiations that
the environmental protection measures stiDulated in the EAP with respect to
the Hanazhou-Ninabo Highway would be carried out in a manner satisfactory to
the Bank.
3.23      Road Develoument Proaram.  Since most of the works under the RDP
would be directed to the upgrading of facilities and rehabilitation and main-
tenance, they are not expected to have a significant impact on the environ-
ment. Nevertheless, the potential adverse effect of each subproject on the
environment would be fully assessed. The environmental assessment would be
carried out under the direction of ZPHAB with the assistance of Zhejiang Pro-
vincial Environmental Protection Bureau (ZPEPB), ZPEPRI and the respective
municipal, prefectural and county highway administration units involved in the
RDP, as outlined in Appendix 2 of Annex 1. These units would be supported
under the project with environmental-related training and equipment as
required.



- 26 -
3.24      For subprojects with an established risk of adverse impact on the
environment, an EA would be carried out and an EAP would be prepared. For
*ubprojects for which the initial environmental screening established that
there is no potential environmental risk, an environmental code of practice
would be applied with regard to the actions to be taken in the design and
implementation stages, and the actions involved would be summarized in an
abbreviated EAP. Regarding these procedures, an understanding has been
reached with ZPG that subproject packages submitted for Bank approval would
include an EAP for each subproject. Assurances were obtained from ZPG at
negotiations that for each subproiect of the RDP the environmental orotection
measures stinulated in the EAPs would be carried out in a manner satisfactory
to the Bank.
E. Land Acquisition anu Resettlement
3.25      Hanazhou-Ningbo Hiahwav.  The Land Administration Law of PRC and
Zhejiang Provincial Land Administration Regulations provide the legal basis
for planning and implementing any land acquisition and resettlement actions
required for the project. Land acquisition and resettlement is well regulated
in China and the procedures and compensation requirements, in general, conform
to the Bank's policies on involuntary resettlement. A Resettlement Action
Plan (RAP) for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway, prepared by ZPEPEC, has been
reviewed by Bank staff and was found to be satisfactory. The approximately
13,000 people being affected by the construction of the highway wili be com-
pensated and resettled. The resettlement proceas is administered by the local
governments at the city/county, township and village levels, in accordance
with the terms of agreements signed between ZPEPEC and the four cities and
three counties affected by the highway alignment. The objective for the
resettled population is to keep the residents in productive employment through
either reallocation of land or the provision of employment in nearby enter-
prises. The implementation of the plan is well advanced and all land acquisi-
tion and resettlement actions are scheduled to be completed by the end of May,
1992. A summary of the RAP for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway and its status is
presented in Annex 3. A copy of the RAP is available in the Project File.
Assurances were obtained from ZPG at negotiations that all land acauisition
and resettlement activities with resDect to the Hantzhou-Ningbo Highway would
be completed according to the RAP in a manner satisfactory to the Bank.
3.26      Road Development Program.  A primary assessment of the RDP was car-
ried out on the basis of the 33 tentatively selected subprojects. This
assessment indicated that about 12,700 individuals could require resettlement
and compensation under the program. An understanding was reached with ZPTD
during project preparation that the land acquisition, resettlement and compen-
sation procedures to be followed would be the same as those applied for the
Hangzhou-Ningbo highway, and subproject packages submitted for Bank approval
would include a RAP for each subproject. Assurances were obtained from ZPG at
neaotiations that the RAPs for subDroiects under the RDP would be carried out
in a manner satisfactorv to the Bank.
3.27      The implementation of the RAPs for both the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway
and the subprojects included in the RDP would be monitored through routine
project supervision and quarterly progress reporting (para. 3.50). The effec-
tiveness and socioeconomic impact of resettlement on the population also would



- 27 -
be evaluated after a certain time interval following the resettlement actions.
Regarding this, assurances were obtained from ZPG at negotiations that. fol-
lowing further consultation with the Bank. Plans to monitor the effectiveness
and socioeconomic impact of the resettlement would be Prepared by ZPG by
June 30. 1993. and such glans would be carried out thereafter as agreed with
the Bank.
F. Cost Estimates
3.28      The project is estimated to cost Y 3.001 billion, or $558.8 million
including land acquisition and physical and price contingencies. A Project
Cost Summary is presented on page 28. The foreign exchange cost is estimated
at $231.44 million, or about 41 percent. Base costs are estimated in December
1991 prices. Physical contingencies are calculated at 10 percent of the base
cost of works and supervision of the highway construction. Price contingen-
cies have been calculated for both foreign and local costs, using interna-
tional annual escalation factors of 1.8 percent in 1992, 1.9 percent in 1993,
3.9 percent in 1994, 4.9 percent in 1995, 4.2 percent in 1996 and 3.6 percent
in 1997, and price escalation factors for the Yuan of 6.0 percent in 1992, and
5 percent thereafter. Identifiable taxes and duties amount to Y 96.7 million
($18.0 million).
3.29      The cost of civil works is based on work quantities calculated from
final engineering designs. The costs were computed on the basis of HOC's
standard manuals for labor and equipment productivity and the related
annually-updated costs and current market costs of materials. The unit rates
were checked against the unit prices provided in recent bids for similar high-
way works executed with Bank financing.
3.30      The cost of equipment was estimated based on prevailing prices and
prices quoted by manufacturers and suppliers. The estimated cost of consult-
ing services is based on recent contracts for similar services under ongoing
highway projects.
G. Financing
3.31      The Bank loan of $220 million represents about 39 percent of the
total project cost or about 95 percent oi the foreign exchange cost. The
Central Government of the People's Republic of China (PRC) would provide $81.2
million equivalent, and the ZPG would provide $257.6 million equivalent to
cover the remaining costs of the project, including contingencies. The pro-
posed onlending terms for the loan between the Central Government and ZPG are:
20 years, including 5 years of grace, at 6 percent interest per annum with a
0.75 percent per annum commitment fee on unwithdrawn balance. The ZPG would
bear the foreign exchange risks. Agreement was reached with the PRC at neRo-
tiations that it would onlend the proceeds of the loan to ZPG under
arranfements satisfactory to the Bank. The making of the onlendinz arranae-
ments would be a condition of loan effectiveness. The details of project
financing are shown on page 29.

H. Implementation
3.32      The implementation of the project would be the responsibility of
ZPG. ZPEPEC would be the Executing Agency for implementing the Hangzhou-
Ningbo Highway, under the direction of the Zhejiang Provincial Shanghai-
Hangzhou-Ningbo Expressway Project Steering Committee. Since the new highway
is a segment of the national highway retwork, MOC would also provide general
supervision and guidance for execution of the project. Invitation to bid was
issued to prequalified contractors in early February 1992. The highway would
be constructed under seven unit-priced civil works contracts. Construction of
the highway under six ICB contracts is scheduled to commence in October 1992
and be completed by the end of 1996, in about 50 months. The works under the
Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) contract are scheduled to commence in Septem-
ber 1992 and be completed in two years. The installation of electrical, elec-
tronic and mechanical systems for the operation of the expressway is scheduled
to commence in mid-1995 and be completed by the end of 1996.  The construction
of service facilities along the highway would be a continuous operation taking
four and a half years.
3.33      The organizational and staffing arrangements for construction super-
vision of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway were formulated during project prepara-
tion. Construction will be supervised by a Joint Supervision Team (JST)
located in Hangzhou and headed by a Chief Supervision Engineer. A Resident
Engineer will be located on site for each of the contracts. Resident engi-
neers will report to the Chief Supervision Engineer's Office through two Engi-
neer's Representative Offices located in Shaoshing and Ningbo. About 160
personnel will be permanently engaged on supervision. The selection of local
personnel and foreign consultants for the team is under way. Invitations to
foreign shortlisted firms were lssued in mid-December 1991 and the evaluation
of proposals is scheduled to be completed by mid-May 1992. The consultants
are expected to be mobilized in August 1992 to begin training local personnel



- 30 -
in construction supervision and to help set up the organization for supevvi-
eion.
3.34      ZPHAB will be the Executing Agency for the RDP.  A special unit has
been set up in ZPHAB to manage the program, coordinate its activities with all
concerned agencies, ensure consistency in the quality of design, supervise,
expedite implementation and meet reporting requirements (Annex 1). The needs
of this unit for laboratory equipment have been included in the list of equip-
ment to be provided under the project. Also, its needs for transport would be
provided under the civil works contracts for the implementation of the RDP.
The ZPTDI, in cooperation with the relevant design institutes of municipali-
ties and prefectures, would be responsible for preparing preliminary and final
engineering designs as well as bidding documents for the subprojects under the
RDP. The design and bidding documents would be reviewed and approved by
ZPHAB. ZPHAB was the principal agency for implementing the Zhejiang Provin-
cial component of the first Bank-financed Highway Project. ZPHAB's perfor-
mance was satisfactory. Construction of the seven subprojects included in the
first package of the program is scheduled to commence in mid-1992 and be com-
pleted in 18 to 30 months. The design and evaluation of subprojects to be
included in subsequent packages of the RDP would be submitted to the Bank for
approval sequentially at about six-month intervals. The program as a whole is
scheduled to be completed by the end of 1996.
3.35      Construction of the subprojects will be supervised by the transport
bureaus at the county and municipal levels, under the overall supervision of
the ZPHAB. These units are capable of supervising the works but their labora-
tory testing facilities need strengthening. To remedy this deficiency, labo-
ratory equipment would be purchased under the project. Assurances were
obtained from ZPG at nexotiations that ZPHAB would establish and maintain a
RDP Unit within ZPHAB with aualified and experienced Rersonnel to provide
overall manatement and sunervision for RDP and that the transport administra-
tions of the respective prefectural/municipal and county governments would be
adeauately staffed with qualified and exoerienced Personnel and provide other
necessary resources for the preparation and execution of the RDP suborolect.
3.36      The responsibility for procurement of equipment would be divided
between the two executing agencies. ZPEPEC would be responsible for the pro-
curement of equipment related to the Hangzhou-Ningbo highway component, such
as equipment for the central laboratory, environmental protection, soft-soil
treatment research, and operation and maintenance of the highway. Equipment
for the central laboratory and environmental protection would be procured in
the second half of 1992 and first half of 1993, equipment for operation and
maintenance of the highway is scheduled to be procured during 1995 and 1996.
3.37      Responsibility for the procurement of equipment related to the RDP
component of the project would rest with ZPHAB. Survey and laboratory equip-
ment would be procured early in project implementation, and maintenance equip-
ment would be procured shortly afterward. Equipment for the RDB and PMS is
scheduled to be obtained during 1993.
3.38      The project as a whole is scheduled to be completed at the end of
1996 with the loan closing date stipulated as June 30, 1998. The implementa-
tion schedule for the project is presented in Table 3.5.

3.40      Procurement arrangements for the project have taken into account the
experience gained from previous Bank-financed highway projects. The documen-
tation for procurement, which covers the prequalification of contractors and
ICB and LCB for civil works and goods, has recently been standardized for
Chinese conditions in cooperation between the Chinese Government and the Bank.
The new set of model documents will be used for all relevant procurement pro-
cesses under the project. In particular, prequalification criteria for civil
works under ICB procedures have been tightened and are now based on perfor-
mance requirements pertinent to each of the contracts. Under previous highway
projects, the Bank accepted force account for minor and scattered civil works
because of the scarcity of construction industry units in remote areas of the
country. However, during recent years a sufficient number of contracting
units have become capable of undertaking road improvement works following a
competitive bidding process. Since the capacity of the contracting units is
still limited, providing further opportunities for local construction units
under this project would enhance technical development and growth of the
industry.



- 32 -
3.41      Three different procurement methods would be applied for the
Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway.
(a)  Six of the seven contracts for the construction of the Hangzhou-
Ningbo Highway, ranging from $44.0 million to $78 million, would be
awarded following ICB procedures at an estimated contract value of
$374.9 million. Contractors, according to their bidding capacity
(determined during the evaluation of the prequalification submis-
sions), could submit bids for one or more contracts. ICB procedures
would also be followed for the supply and installation of electri-
cal, electronic and mechanical equipment required for the toll
facilities, traffic monitoring, telecommunication services and
lighting.
(b)  One civil works contract for the Highway would be awarded following
LCB procedures at an estimated contract value of $9.1 million. The
contract covers the construction of a 700 m section on the western
end of the Highway and the Pengbu interchange, linked to the already
completed 7 km section of the highway (para. 3.8). The construction
of this 700 m section has been accorded high priority to facilitate
traffic flow between Xaoshing and Hangzhou and to take advantage of
the already contracted 7 km section of the highway.
(c)  The contracts for the construction of service facilities, comprising
buildings for toll stations, maintenance depots, service and rest
areas and administration and associated minor civil works along the
highway, would be awarded under local shopping procedures acceptable
to the Bank. Because of the scattered nature of these facilities
and the relatively small scale and dispersed nature of the local
building industry, this method of procurement was considered appro-
priate for contracts not exceeding $2.0 million, and in an aggregate
not exceeding $5.8 million. The contract for the main administra-
tion building, with an estimated amount of $4.0 million, would be
procured following LCB procedures.
3.42      Under the RDP the construction and improvement of about 33 road
sections is being planned for implementation in a number of packages over a
period of about four and a half years. These roads are widely dispersed in
the southern region of the Province and were considered for procurement as
follows:
(a)  Contracts exceeding the estimated contract amount of $3.75 million
would be awarded following LCB procedures at an estimated total
contract value of $53.7 million; and
(b)  Contracts at an estimated contract amount below $3.75 million would
be awarded under local shopping procedures acceptable to the Bank;
of the 33 road sections 25 sections are expected to fall under this
category with an aggregated contract amount not exceeding $42.0
million.
3.43      Major construction and maintenance equipment would be procured fol-
lowing ICB procedures. However, all other items or groups of items covering



- 33 -
minor maintenance equipment, equipment for RDB, PMS, laboratories, research
and environmental protection purposes estimated to cost les than $200,000 per
contract, in an aggregate amount not exceeding $3.0 million, may be procured
following international or local shopping procedures on the basis of a com-
parison of price quotations solicited from at least three suppliers eligible
under the Bank Guidelines for Procurement.
3.44      Under ICB, qualifying domestic contractors and manufacturers would
be eligible for a margin of preference in the comparison of bids of 7.5 per-
cent for civil works and 15 percent for goods, or the prevailing custom
duties, whichever is lower. Civil works contracts at a value exceeding $3.75
million and contracts for the procurement of goods exceeding $200,000 would be
subject to prior review and agreement to procurement documentation by the
Bank. This would represent about 87 percent of the estimated amount of all
contracts under the project.
3.45      The need for foreign consulting expertise was identified for the
supervision of construction and staff training, although local expertise
available in the various provincial universities and institutes in the subsec-
tor would be used as much as possible. All consultants would be employed
under the terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank in accordance with Bank
Guidelines on the Use of Consultants.
J. Disbursements
3.46      Disbursements of the Loan would cover:
(a)  41 percent of total expenditures for highway civil works;
(b) 70 percent of total expenditures for the supply and installation of
E&M equipment for the operation of the highway;
(c) 34 percent of total expenditures for the Road Development Program;
(d) 100 percent of foreign expenditures for directly-imported equipment
or 100 percent of local expenditures (ex-factory) for locally manu-
factured equipment and 75 percent of local expenditures for other
items procured locally; and
(e) 100 percent of expenditures for consulting services and staff train-
ing.
3.47      To facilitate disbursement, a Special Account would be opened with
an initial deposit in US dollars of $14.0 million equivalent, the estimated
average expenditure for a four-month period. The account would be opened in
US dollars in a bank acceptable to the Bank, and would be managed by ZPG.
Applications for replenishment of the Special Account would be submitted
monthly or whenever the Special Account is drawn down to 50 percent of its
initial deposit, whichever comes first. A schedule of estimated disbursements
is given in Table 3.6. This schedule takes into consideration the disburse-
ment profile for transport projects in China and the potential risk of delays
in project implementation due to the difficult subsoil conditions.



- 34 -
3.48      Disbursements would be made against priced contracts for the civil
works and delivery and installation of the equipment. Interim certification
of civil works completed and costed at unit rates in the contracts will be
done by the JST, and payments will be authorized by ZPEPEC and ZPHAB. Dis-
bursements for staff training in China or abroad will be made against the
actual costs of travel, subsistence, and tuition or training fees. Reimburse-
ment for expenditures related to training, and on contracts for works and
goods individually valued at $200,000 and less would be made on the basis of
statements of expenses. Documentation supporting SOEs would be retained by
the implementing agencies and would be made available for review by Bank
supervision missions.
K. Auditing
3.49      The Foreign Investment Audit Bureau of the State Audit Administra-
tion (SAA) will be responsible for auditing the accounts of project expendi-
tures. Actual audits would be carried out either by the Zhejiang Branch of
SAM or the Zhejiang Provincial Audit Administration. Assurances were obtained
from ZPG during negotiations that project accounts and the Special Account.
including statements of expenditures, would be audited by inde2endent auditors
acceptable to the Bank and that audit reports would be sent to the Bank for
review within six months of the close of each fiscal year.
L.  Proiect Sunervision. Reporting. Monitorina
3.50      The project would be supervised regularly by Bank missions.  A
supervision mission plan, indicating key activities, is presented in Annex 4.
In addition to field missions, staff input would be provided at headquarters
for project-related activities such as completion of supervision reports,
review of documentation submitted for the clearance of RADP subproject pack-
ages, review of procurement documentation and contracts, and staff training
proposals. The Bank's supervision input is estimated at 60 staff-weeks, 40 in
the field and 20 at headquarters. In addition to field supervision, the prog-
ress of works also would be monitored through quarterly progress reports which
would be submitted to the Bank by the project executing agencies. A copy of a
Quarterly Progress Report Outline is available in the Project File (Annex 5).
A Project Completion Report would be prepared and submitted to the Bank not
later than six months after the loan closing date.
IV. ECONOMIC EVALUATION
4.1       The economic analysis for the proposed project focuses on the civil
works components, which account for 94 percent of the project base cost, and
comprise two major subcomponents:
(a) construction of the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway; and
(b) a Road Development Program.
4.2       The economic analysis was prepared using 1991 financial costs, modi-
fied by appropriate conversion factors to represent the economic costs.



- 35 -
A. Main Benefits and Beneficiaries
4.3       The major quantifiable benefit. of the project include:  (i) a
decrease in traffic congestion along the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway, through the
provision of additional traffic capacity on a new, parallel highway alignment
and reduced traffic distances due to the direct nature of the new alignment;
and (ii) improvements in the condition and capacity of provincial and county
highways and bridges at several locations in the poorer part. of the Province,
as well as the construction of some new roads to provide motorized access to
communities, and some new bridges to replace either ferry service, or inade-
quate existing bridges.
4.4       Benefits from the project would accrue to road users in the form of
reduced vehicle operating costs for the own-account operators, farmers and
state enterprises in particular, time savings to the goods carriers as well as
passengers, and to the ZPG in the form of reduced highway maintenance costs.
4.5       The project is intended to encourage better highway managefflant and
maintenance through the development of the RDB and PMS, and through the provi-
sion of modern maintenance equipment. The project would also lead to the
strengthening of the public works procurement procedures and to improved man-
agement.
B   Hantzhou-Ningbo Hishwav
4.6       The Existina Corridor.  The corridor linking Hangzhou and Ningbo is
the economic focus in Zhejiang Province, currently accounting for about half
the GOVAI, and projected to account for 60 percent of GOVAI by the year 2000
(Table 4.1). In 1989, the pcpulation in the corridor was 14.9 million
(35 percent of the Provincial total), with 2.4 million living in the main
cities of Hangzhou, Shaoxing and Ningbo. The projected economic growth of
approximately 7.5 percent per year will place increasing demands on the corri-
dor's transport system.
4.7       The corridor is served by highway, rail lines, and waterway.  The
main highway comprises National Route 104 between Hangzhou, Shaoxing, and
Shangyu (95 km) and National Route 329 between Shangyu and Ningbo (109 km).
The corridor is intensively developed, with about one third of National Route
104 urban in nature. Access to Hangzhou is particularly difficult, because of
an old and narrow bridge, and vehicle breakdowns are common and difficult to
clear. The 109 km corridor of National Route 329 from Shangyu to Ningbo via
Cixi is less intensely developed, although the highway passes through many
villages. National Route 329 is currently being upgraded to a 9 meter pave-
ment.
4.8       The corridor is also served by the Xiaoyung Railway, which links
Hangzhou with Beilun Port at Ningbo. It is currently a single-track line,
planned for expansion to a double-track in the early 1990s. Approximately 13
million tons of freight is carried each year between Hangzhou and Shaoxing,
and 8 million tons is carried between Shaoxing and Hangzhou. At Hangzhou, the
Xiaoyung Railway is linked to the Hangzhou-Shanghai line, and to the Zhegan
Railway which links Hangzhou to Jiangxi and Hunan Provinces. The Zhegan Rail-
way is currently being double-tracked.



- 36 -
4.9       The first part of the corridor between Hangzhou, Xiaoshan, Shaoxing
and Shangyu is served by the Grand Canal, which is linked to the Yangtze River
and is navigable by 500-2,000 ton barges. The Grand Canal at Shangyu is con-
tinued in the Hangyung Canal to Ningbo, but capacity there is limited to ves-
sels under 40 tons.
4.10      Traffic on the Existing Roads.  Traffic on the existing roads has
grown rapidly in recent years. Provincial counting records indicate that
between 1982 and 1991, volumes increased almost four times in the Hangzhou-
Shangyu section, and between five and six times in the Shangyu-Ningbo section
(Table 4.2). Over the past two years the traffic growth has continued to be
high, estimated at over 10 percent annually.
4.11      Most of the road traffic comprises trucks, buses and (to a much
lesser extent) cars, but there are also significant volumes of agricultural
tractors, especially in the sections closer to Ningbo, as well as bicycle
traffic. Because of the high traffic volumes, the highway is susceptible to
congestion, particularly in the event of maintenance works, traffic accidents,
and bad weather.
4.12      The ProDosed Hangzhou-Ningbo Highwal.  The highway is proposed as a
four-lane divided highway with access restricted to normal motor traffic;
agricultural tractors and nonmotorized traffic would be excluded. Four align-
ments were examined by ZPTDI, and the option chosen was confirmed by a review
of the alternatives conducted in 1990 by Louis Berger International. The
selected alignment parallels the existing National Route 104 to the north
between Hangzhou and Shangyu, crossing National Route 329 between Shangyu and
Ningbo, and reachiig Ningbo on a more direct alignment south of Route 329.
The new highway would offer significant distance savings, 22 kilometers from
Hangzhou to Shangyu and 37 kilometers from Shangyu to Ningbo, which accounts
for approximately 23 percent and 34 percent of the existing distance, respec-
tively (Map 2).
4.13      Proiected Traffic on the Pronosed Hiahwav.  The traffic projections
for the proposed new highway consider diversion of traffic from the existing
highway, diversion of both freight and passengers from the existing railway,
new traffic likely to be generated by the port expansion at Ningbo, and gener-
ated traffic expected to be stimulated by the transport improvements offered
by the new highway (Table 4.3). Traffic volumes are expected to be highest on
the Hangzhou-Shangyu sections at between 24,000 to 28,000 passenger car units
(pcus) per day in 1997 (year of opening), dropping to 16,000 to 17,000 pcus/
day for the Shangyu-Ningbo sections in the same year. Traffic growth rates
were projected at between 6 and 7 percent per year, and are quite conservative
when compared with the rates experienced in the last few years.
4.14      Economic Analysis.  Project cost and benefit estimates are based on
the 1988/89 feasibility study conducted by ZPTDI, the review report of Louis
Berger International in January 1991, and further updating conducted by Bank
staff during appraisal.
4.15      Total financial costs of construction for the project are estimated
at Y 2,437 million, including allowances for physical and price contingencies.
Economic costs of Y 2,443 million were estimated from the financial costs by



- 37 -
deducting taxes and price contingencies, and adjusting by a factor of 1.20 to
reflect the currently estimated (1991) shadow price of project components
(Table 4.4). The average construction cost (economic and financial) of nearly
Y 18 million per kilometer is quite high compared with previous Bank-financed
highway projects; this reflects, among other things, the difficult soft-soil
conditions prevalent in the region and the higher than average need for major
structures.
4.16      The highway is assumed to take four years to construct, opening in
1997. In some sections of the route with soft-soil conditions, additional
costs are anticipated five years after opening to repair expected settlement
damage and to overlay the pavement. Additional costs are anticipated 14 years
after opening for pavement overlays in all sections of the highway. Annual
costs of highway maintenance and toll operations have also been included.
4.17      Project benefits were estimated based primarily on savings in travel
time and vehicle operating costs. Average vehicle operating costs by vehicle
class, pavement type, pavement condition, and terrain are shown in Table 4.5.
These estimates of vehicle operating costs were increased for congested high-
ways to reflect lower traffic speeds and greater frequency of speed-cHtange
cycles prevailing under these conditions. Only 25 percent of the estimated
project benefits for the first section of route between the Pengbu and
Qiantang interchanges was used in the evaluation; the unused portion was
assumed to be attributable to the Qiantang Bridge which was completed in Janu-
ary 1992 with local financing.
4.18      The highway was first evaluated without tolls, using the discounted
cost-benefit analysis shown in Table 4.6. For this case, construction costs
were reduced by Y 53 million to eliminate toll booth costs and the recurrent
costs of toll operations. The estimated economic internal rate of return
(EIRR) was over 22 percent, with a first year rate of return (PYRR) in 1997 of
nearly 19 percent. Economic returns are generally higher in the more heavily
trafficked western section of the freeway near Hangzhou, with the lowest
ranked section still achieving an ElR of nearly 18 percent.
4.19      Tol1_ig.  It is intended that the highway be operated as a toll
road, which will involve higher construction and operation costs, due to the
toll facilities, and a potential loss of estimated benefits. Experience from
tolled roads in China has been inconsistent to date. In the north, traffic
levels have been lower than expected, generating disappointing toll revenues.-
In the south, experience on the Guangzhou-Foshan Highway in Guangdong Province
was more encouraging: after a slow start following opening in August 1989,
revenues are now reported at around Y 100,000 per day, or Y 36 million per
year, compared with an investment cost of Y 280 million. The relative pros-
perity of the corridor in which the Hangzhou-Ningbo toll road is located sug-
gests that the Guangdong Province experience provides a better model for pro-
jecting toll road operations. Since tolling will impact the project benefits,
but this impact cannot at present be determined with reasonable confidence, an
assurance was obtained from ZPG at netotiations. that. takinR into considera-
tion the prevailing tollint exDerience in China, it would submit recommenda-
tions for the Bank's review by June 30. 1996 regarding the apDropriate toll
levels for the Hanazhou-Nintbo Hiahwav.



- 38 -
4.20      Table 4.7 sets out the discounted cost-benefit analysis for toll
highway operations. The imposition of toll would have the effect of reducing
traffic volumes along the highway but long-distance and short-distance traffic
would be affected differently. Long-distance traffic using the new highway
would be less affected, since the savings offered by the new highway compared
to the existing road are considerable and would not easily be offset by tolls.
For shorter distance local traffic, perhaps using one or two sections of the
new highway, the choice of the new highway depends on the delicate balance
between shorter, but congested, travel on the existing road versus longer
distances (including travel on access roads) but less congested travel via the
new highway. The tolling of the new highway will set the balance point
between the old and the new route, and could be expected to deter short dis-
tance traffic from using the new highway; the extent of such deterrence would
depend to a large degree on the level of tolling. Since the benefits from the
new highway accrue mostly to longer distance traffic, it can be expected that
the drop in traffic on the new highway due to the imposition of tolls is
likely to be greater than the drop in benefits. The evaluation assumes that
traffic volumes under tolled conditions (with tolls assumed to be set at no
more than 30 percent of attributable cost savings) would reduce by about
17 percent, but that benefits would reduce by only 14 percent on average,
reflecting the fact that marginal beneficiaries of the nontolled highway oper-
ation are likely to be more easily deterred by tolls. The evaluation with
tolls indicates only a small reduction in economic performance, with the aver-
age EIRR reduced to 20 percent and the FYRR to just below 16 percent.
4.21      Sensitivity Tests.  Sensitivity tests (without tolls) indicate that
the project would remain viable if construction costs rose by 10 percent and
benefits dropped by the same proportion (Table 4.8). The first-year rate of
return (FYRR) of the section between Shangyu and Yuyao would fall just below
10 percent under these circumstances, but this is within the scope of the
opportunity cost of capital in China (about 10 percent). Combining the intro-
duction of tolls with a 10 percent increase in construction costs and 10 per-
cent decrease in benefits still produces an overall EIRR over 17 percent
(Table 4.9).
C. Road DeveloDment Proaram
4.22      The Road Development Program is intended to improve and develop
provincial and county roads, and comprises a series of small-scale subprojects
each taking about two to three years to construct. It will be implemented on
a program basis, with subprojects evaluated individually using the methodology
presented in Annex 1, and summarized below.
4.23      Traffic.  Traffic on the roads proposed for financing under the RDP
have been assessed by converting tractors and other nonmotorized vehicles to a
4-ton medium truck equivalent. The 1990 daily motorized traffic was observed
to vary between 270-5,300 vpd on the evaluated subprojects.
4.24      Taking into consideration the characteristics of the specific areas
of the subprojects, a uniform set of traffic growth rates was developed and
used in the evaluations, as follows:



- 39 -
ANNUAL GROWTH RATE
Traffic Type       1991-95       1996-2000      2001-10
Vehicles              102           8X              5Z
Tractors              5Z            22             OZ
Others              2.52            02            -52
4.25      Economic Analysis of the First Packame.  The first package includes
subprojects from each of the four categories identified, with benefits accru-
ing in the following form:
(a) reduced vehicle operating costs on upgraded roads due to improved
geometrics, improved surfaces, and reduced travel distances;
(b) reduced travel time and congestion costs due to road upgrading,
enlargement of bottlenecks, and replacement of ferries by bridges;
and
(c) economic development benefits in areas which are opened to motor
vehicle traffic for the first time.
4.26      The economic evaluations for each subproject of the first package
are set out in detail in Annex 1. The following description identifies the
main steps followed for each category of subprojects.
Cateaory A and B. Uotaradina and Bottleneck Suburoiects.  Savings in vehi-
cle operating costs were the main benefits attributed to this category.
It was found that these costs generally reduce with the subproject due to
better pavement surfaces, reduced travel distances, and lower (but per-
haps not eliminated) congestion costs. A comparison of the with and
without subproject cases identified the benefits of the subproject, sepa-
rately for normal traffic (traffic without the new subproject) and gener-
ated traffic (the traffic additional to normal growth). The benefita,
estimated by year, were then compared with the costs of construction and
maintenance to establish a net stream of costs and benefits. Standard
economic evaluation measures of Net Present Value (NPV), EIRR, and FYRR
were then calculated.
Cateaorv C. Bridge SubDroiects. The bridge subprojects in the first
package, discussed in detail in Annex 1, include the following identified
benefits:
(a) elimination of ferry operating costs;
(b) savings in vehicle operating costs from the elimination of long
detours in the period. during the year when the ferry could not
operate due to unusually high or low water conditions;



- 40 -
(c)  generated traffic benefits for traffic operations which normally
cease when the ferry cannot operate;
(d)  savings in passenger time, and improved vehicle utilization, by
avoiding the delays associated with ferry operations; and
(e)  reductions in congestion costs resulting from a bypass of the inade-
quate existing bridge.
Cateaorv D. Penetration Roads Subgroiects. The main benefits from these
subprojects were derived by replacing human porterage with motorized
transport. There are two main subcomponents: (i) the direct cost sav-
ings from using motorized traffic for existing transport needs (including
normal growth); and (ii) the benefits from likely additional traffic
generated by the sharp reduction in transport costs brought about by
motorized transport. Quite conservative assumptions were made on the
latter; better estimates would require expert evaluation of the agricul-
tural and mineral resources of each region and their potential markets.
Additional benefits were identified for traffic attracted to the new
penetration road which offers a shortcut for existing motorized traffic
using other routes.

ects are summarized as follows:

D. Overall Evaluation and Risks
4.28      The comparison of benefits and costs yields an EIRR of approximately
22 percent for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway, without tolling, and 26 percent
for the first package of the RDP. With the tolling of the highway there will
be additional costs for its construction and operation, and a potential of
lost benefits as some operators opt to use untolled routes. However, based on
Bank studies and experience elsewhere in the world, an appropriate toll level
would result in limited traffic diversion so that the EIRR would remain
acceptable (in this case it would be about 20 percent), and would produce
revenues sufficient to cover at least the operating costs of maintenance.



- 41 -
4.29      Because of the difficult subsoil conditions along the alignment of
the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway, the major risk is delay in the completion of the
highway and the quality of construction. Since ZPEPEC is a first-time execut-
ing agency for a Bank project, there is also a risk in implementing the proj-
ect in a timely manner. However, the preparation of the project was assisted
by the Bank's experience in previous Chinese highway projects, and by the more
active involvement of the Ministry of Communications; this enhanced role of
MOC is expected to continue into future projects. To minimize the risks asso-
ciated with the subsoil conditions, the project was designed with the assis-
tance of foreign experts, the prequalification of contractors was made partic-
ularly stringent, and supervision arrangements were formulated to ensure
regular monitoring and effective quality control.
V. AGREEMENT REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
5.1       Agreement was reached with PRC at negotiations that it would onlend
the proceeds of the loan to ZPG under arrangements satisfactory to the Bank
(para.3.31).
5.2       During negotiations, assurances were obtained from ZPG that:
(a) subprojects to be included in the RDP would be selected in accord-
ance with criteria and procedures satisfactory to the Bank and would
be submitted to the Bank for approval (para. 3.15);
(b) the environmental protection measures stipulated in the LAP with
respect to the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway would be carried out in a
manner satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.22);
(c) for each subproject of the RDP the environmental protection measures
stipulated in the EAPs would be carried out in a manner satisfactory
to the Bank (para. 3.24);
(d)  all land acquisition and resettlement activities with respect to the
Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway would be completed according to the RAP in a
manner satisfactory to the Bank (3.25);
(e) the RAPs for subprojects under the RDP would be carried out in a
manner satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.26);
(f) following further consultation with the Bank, plans to monitor the
effectiveness and socioeconomic impact of resettlement would be
prepared by June 30, 1993, and such plans would be carried out
thereafter as agreed with the Bank (para. 3.27);
(g)  ZPHAB would establish and maintain a RDP Unit within ZPHAB with
qualified and experienced personnel to provide overall management
and supervision for RDP and that the transport administrations of
the respective prefectural/municipal and county governments would be
adequately staffed with qualified and experienced personnel and



- 42 -
provide other necessary resources for the preparation and execution
of the RDP subprojects (para. 3.35);
(h) the project accounts and the Special Account, including statements
of xpenditures, would be audited by independent auditors acceptable
to the Bank and that audit reports would be sent to the Bank for
review within six months of the close of each fiscal year (para.
3.49)t and
(i) taking into consideration the prevailing tolling experience in
China, ZPG would submit recommendations for the Bank's review by
June 30, 1996 regarding the appropriate toll levels for the
Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway (para. 4.19).
5.3       The making of the onlending arrangements satisfactory to the Bank
would be a condition of loan effectiveness (para. 3.31).
5.4       The approval of subprojects for the RDP bv the Bank would be a con-
dition of disbursement (para. 3.15).
5.5       Subject to the foregoing, the proposed project would be suitable for
a Bank loan of $220 million to the People's Republic of China. The loan would
be for a term of 20 years, including a grace period of five years, at the
Bank's standard variable interest rate.

CHINA
ZHEJIANG PROVINCIAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
Road Develoyment Proaram
A. Introduction
1.        The southern part of Zhejiang Province is the most underdeveloped
region of the province. Most roads in the region have low geometric standards
and poor surface conditions and several counties do not have any asphalt-paved
roads. In some areas, portering and animal-drawn carts are still the major
means of freight transportation. Lack of proper road infrastructure is one of
the major obstacles to improving the living standards of those who live in
this region.
2.        The experiences from the first Bank-financed Highway Project in
China showed that significant changes have been brought by the rural road
improvement program. "The opening up of all-veather access to areas that had
previously relied on porter or rough tracks has resulted in substantial
increases in the standard of living of those within the influence zone of the
new roads, the creation of new industries or the revitalizing of existing ones
and the exploitation of mineral deposits that vere formerly uneconomic" (High-
way Project, Draft Project Completion Report, January 1992).
B. The Proaram
3.        The Road Development Program would include the construction and/or
improvement of about 970 km of provincial and county roads. Although their
nature varies from subproject to subproject, the improvements can be catego-
rized into four groups which are listed as follows:
Group At  full-scale upgrading, in which geometry and pavement would be
upgraded for the entire length of the roads;
Group Bt bottleneck relief, in which only those sections with bottle-
necks would be upgraded. This usually involves widening the
pavement, and realigning or constructing bypasses to towns;
Group Cs new bridge construction, in which new bridges would be built to
replace a ferry or an existing low-standard bridge;
Group Dt new penetration road construction, in which new access roads
would be built in the areas where no motorable road exists.
4.        ZPG identified 33 subprojects with a total investment of Y 562 mil-
lion to be Included in the RDP, as follows (the detailed cost table is pre-
sented in Appendix 1 to this annex)s



77 -                             ANNEX 1
Page 2
Length         Financial cost
Group        Project type          Number           (km)           (Y million)
A        Upgrading                  11            465                 204
B        Bottleneck Relief          5             201                 145
C        Bridge                     4              11                  58
D        Access Road                13            293                 155
Total               33             970                 562
5.        The RDP will be processed in a series of packages.  Only the sub-
projects in the first package have been appraised, but the evaluation of sub-
projects in subsequent packages will follow the methodology outlined in this
annex. The subprojects selected for inclusion in the subsequent packages will
depend on the status of preparation of the required studies and the availabil-
ity of loce' funds, end other criteria stipulated in para. 6 of this annex.
Most of the tentatively identified 33 subprojects are expected to qualify for
inclusion in the RDP; however, other subprojects meeting selection criteria
could be added to the program*
C. Criteria for Subproiect Selection
6.        The criteria for obtaining the Bank'. concurrence to include sub-
projects in the RDP would be as follows:
(a)  The Economic Internal Rate of Return (EIRR), calculated according to
methodology presented in paras. 24-62 of this annex for category A,
B, C and D type subprojects, would not be lose than 12 percent.
(b)  Engineering design would be prepared according to the geometric
standards indicated in Table 1.7 and sound engineering practices.
(c)  The procurement procedures would be in conformity with Bank Guide-
lines for Procurement, specifically paras. 2 and 3 of Appendix I
thereof.
(d)  For each subproject with potential adverso impact on the environment
or located in environmentally sensitive areas, an Environmental
Assessment and an Environmental Action Plan, and for all other sub-
projects an Environmental Action Plan only, would be prepared. All
subprojects would undergo an environmental impact analysis as out-
lined in Appendix 2 to this annex.
(e)  A Resettlement Action Plan for each subproject would be prepared in
accordance with the procedures &pplied for the preparation of the
Resettlement Action Plan for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway.



- 78 -                            ANNEX I
Page 3
D. Organizational Arrannements
7,        The Zhejiang Provincial Highway Administration Bureau would be the
executing agency for the RDP at the provincial level. An RDP Unit within
ZPHAB is being eet up for coordinating and supervising the RDP. It will be
headed by a deputy director and staffed with three planners, two to five
supervisors, and two financial administrators. On the municipal/prefectural
level of administration, the transport bureaus would be responsible for the
coordination and management of a corresponding portion of the RDP with a per-
manent staff of between eevea to nine. Similar administrativ;- arrangements
would be made at the county level.
8.        More specifically, the functions of the RDP units at the different
levels of administration would be as followss The RDP Unit in ZPHAB would be
responsible for preparing engineering design, economic evaluation, bidding
documents, and technical specifications and for managing the bidding process,
including bid evaluation. Construction contracts would be signed by the
authorized representative of the RDP Unit. The Unit also would coordinate the
supervision of construction, including organizational arrangements, staffing
and training programs for supervisors, in accordance with the nature of the
works, and the purchasing of equipment for laboratories.
9.        The municipal/prefectural units of the RDP would assign the engi-
neer's representative for supervision, and the county units of the RDP would
assign the resident supervisors for the subprojects. The municipal/prefec-
tural units, in coordination with the county and local government entities,
would be responsible for environmental, land acquisition and resettlement
actions under the guidance of ZPHAB's RDP Unit, the Zhejiang Provincial Envi-
ronmental Protection Bureau, and the Zhejiang Provincial Environmental Protec-
tion Institute.
E. The First Package
10.       The first package includes the following subprojects:  Xlindeng-
Shun'an (A-2), Xiaoshi-Lushan (A-2), Xiangshan-Medlin (A-3), Xiaoshan-Jinhua
(B-4), Bihu Bridge (C-1), Linpu Bridge (C-4) and Xiazhai-Jincun (D-5). The
economic evaluations show that each would yield a high rate of return. As
displayed in the following table, the EIRR for each project varies from 14 to
35 percent, with an overall value of 26 percent.

F. Traffic Forecast
11.       General DescriDtion.  Road traffic was initially projected using the
growth factor method. That is: daily traffic volume in a given year was
estimated by multiplying the traffic volume in the previous year with a cer-
tain growth factor. Traffic volumes were projected for three types of traf-
fic: vehicle, tractor, and others (mainly bicycles). In the evaluation of
Class C projects (bridge/ferry substitutions), passenger volumes were identi-
fied separately to obtain a better estimate of travel-time savings. The esti-
mated traffic volumes were then converted to an equivalent truck traffic vol-
ume and compared with the capacity of the road. If the equivalent volume was
lower than road capacity, the projected volume was taken as the future traffic
volume. If the converted volume was higher than road capacity, it was then
adjusted to equal the capacity of the road, and the volumes by vehicle type
were adjusted proportionately.
12.       Conversion Factor.  The 4-ton medium truck (5.5-ton gross vehicle
weight) was used as the standard vehicle, and other types of traffic were
converted to truck traffic. The conversion factor for vehicles, tractors and
other traffic were assumed to be as follows:
Vehicle            1.0
Tractor            1.0
Others             0.067
13.       Traffic Growth Factor.  Zhejiang Province has experienced rapid
economic growth in recent years, which has impacted growth in highway traffic.
From 1984 to 1990, traffic growth on the highway system was particularly
rapid.  The annual growth rate was about 35 percent for freight (in term of
ton-km) and approaching 14 percent for passengers (in terms of passenger-km).
Although the current pace of economic growth suggests that this growth in
traffic will continue into the future, much more conservative growth rates
were used for the evaluation.



-80 A-E  I
Page 5
14.       A uniform set of traffic growth rates was used in the evaluations of
the subprojects. The growth rates used for various types of traffic over
different time periods are listed in the following table:
Annual Growth Rate
Traffic Type       1991-95       1996-2000      2001-10
Vehicles              101           81              51
Tractors              5S            2Z              0S
Others              2.5Z            01            -51
Vehicle traffic is assumed to grow in all three time periods, but at decreas-
ing rates. Tractor traffic is expected to grow in the near future but at much
lower rates than vehicle traffic. Beyond the year 2000, the tractor traffic
growth rate is set at zero. Other traffic (mainly bicycles) is assumed to
have a negative growth beyond the year 2000 to reflect the likely switch to
motorized vehicles as the province becomes more developed. The growth rates
presented above account for normal traffic growth likely to occur with eco-
nomic development. Additional traffic is assumed to be generated by the proj-
ect at a rate of 10 percent of the normal traffic.
G.  Hlijhwav  Capacitv
15.       Practical CapacitZ.  The practical capacity of a highway is defined
as the maximum traffic volume reached before traffic becomes congested. Con-
gestion effects, in the form of impedance of the faster vehicles by slower
ones, will be felt over the whole range of traffic volumes up to that volume,
but instability of flow and the onset of stop-go conditions will occutr only
after this volume is exceeded.
16.       The practical capacity of a flat, tangent, 7 m wide two-lane highway
is about 2,500 Passenger Car Equivalent (pee) per hour. Since a medium-size
truck is equivalent to 2 passenger cars, practical capacity for this type of
highway is assumed to be 1,250 pee.
17.       Effect of Width.  Capaclty is assumed to vary with the width of
pavement. The following adjustment factors were adopted to modify the capa-
city of a 7 m road (with linear interpolation) when the road width is differ-
ent from 7 mt
7.0 m     1.00
6.7 m     0.98
6.1 m     0.90
5.5 m     0.80
18.       Effect of Grade.  Road capacity drops with Increasing hilliness.
The following factors were adopted to modify capacity for each road geometric
class:



- 81                                  _
Page 6
Flat/Rolling:  0 85
Hilly:         0.65
Mountainous:   0.50
H. Vehicle 0perating Cost
19.       Vehicle operating costs were estimated in two steps.  First, free-
flow operating costs were estimated and then the values were modified to
reflect the degree of traffic congestion.
20.       Free-flow operating costs for various types of vehicles are listed
in Table 4.5. To obtain an average operating cost for vehicle traffic, the
following fleet composition was assumed for vehicle traffic on all roads ana-
lyzed:
Vehicle type             Percentage
Small car                      13
Small bus                      13
Medium bus                      4
Large bus                       4
Small truck                    16
Medium truck                   33
Large truck                     2
Tractor-Trailer                15
The average vehicle operating cost, weighted by the above percentage traffic
composition, was defined as follows:
Surface         Pavement           Flat/rolling        Hilly         Mountainous
Type            Conditions
Paved              Good                0.73             0.88            1.02
Fair               0.81             0.98             1.11
Poor               1.01             1.16             1.30
Unpaved            Good                0.92             1.09            1.32
Fair               1.49             1.88            2.21
Poor               1.71             2.10            2.44
21.       The purchase price of a mechanized hand tractor was estimated at
about one sixth of the cost of a small truck. Running costs are probably not
proportionately as low as the purchase price; therefore, it was assumed that
tractor operating costs are one fourth of the average vehicle operating cost.
22.       Vehicle operating costs under congested conditions were obtained by
multiplying free-flow operating cost by a congestion factor, estimated as a
function of the daily volume capacity ratio. The congestion factors used for
different volume capacity ratios are listed as followst

1.0                                   2.50
I. Road Maintenance Costs
23.       Road maintenance cost. are divided into fixed cost and variable
cost. The fixed cost refers to the expenses incurred when o ly minimal main-
tenance activities are observed, which does not change over traffic volume.
In the evaluations, the fixed cost is assumed to be Y 4,400 per km per year
for roads with asphalt surface and Y 4,700 for gravel and earth roads. The
variable cost changes when the traffic volume changes. It can be measured as
a linear function of daily traffic volume. The variable maintenance costs are
Y 30 and Y 53.5 per vpd/km/year for asphalt surface and gravel surface roads,
respectively. For exa-uple, the annual maintenance cost for a gravel road with
200 vpd would be 4700+53.5x200-Y 15,400/km.
J. Economic Evaluations
24.       The evaluations for each of the seven subprojects in the first pack-
age are presented below. A standard evaluation method was adopted for each
category of subprojects (A, B, C and D) and defined on a computer spreadsheet.
The spreadsheet evaluation results for each of the seven subprojects in the
first package are presented in Appendixes 3-9 to this annex, and are discussed
below. The first subprojects in each category are discussed in a little more
detail to explain the methodology used. The methodology for the Linpu Bridge
subproject (C-4) is also explained more fully since it departs from the stan-
dard methodelogy for the C category subprojects.
The Xindeno-Shun'an Road IA-18
25.       This is a Category A subproject to upgrade an existing gravel road
to asphalt surface with better alignment.
26.       The Road Influence Area.  The road (110 km) runs from Xindeng in
Fuyang County, mouth through Tonglu County to Pailin, the capital of Shun'an
County. The road influence area extends over 2,825 ikm2, covering 3 counties
and 67 villages with a total population of 820,000. The major agricultural
products include rice, tea, timber, fruits and silkworm cocoons. The influ-
ence area is also an important tourist attraction. The GOVAI irF the region
was Y 543 million in 1990.
27.       Existino Road and Traffic Conditions.  The existing road was built
in 1958, when China was enduring severe economic difficulties. As a result,
the initial quality of the road construction was poor. It is currently clas-
sified as Class 4 standard and is paved with a gravel-macadam surface. Some



-83-                               ANNEX I
Page 8
sections of the road have been upgraded recently, and the total length of the
sections which would be upgraded as part of this subproject is 64.4 km. The
subproject includes upgrading road geometric. and paving the surface with
asphalt penetration macadam. The observed daily traffic in 1990 is estimated
as follows:
Vehicles     300
Tractors     175
Others        50
28.       Traffic Forecast.  Traffic growth was estimated according to the
method specified in Section G of this annex. The maximum projected volumes
were below the traffic capacity of the highway, both with and without the
subproject, and so no adjustment were made to reflect capacity constraints.
29.       Proiect Costs and Benefits.  The economic cost of upgrading this
road to Class 1 standards is estimated to be Y 27.3 million (the estimated
financial cost is Y 26.6 million) and the upgrading is expected to take two
year. to complete starting in 1993. The major quantifiable benefits from the
subproject road include savings in vehicle operating costs and road mainte-

The Xiaoshi-Lushan Road (A-2)
32.       This is a Category A subproject to upgrade an existing gravel road
to asphalt surface with improved alignment.
33.       The Road Influence Area. The road (16.1 km) is located in the cen-
tral part of Huzhou Prefecture. It is the only road which links Anji County
with Huzhou City, the capital of the prefecture, and provides access to
Shanghai and Jiangsu. The road runs from the suburbs of Huzhou City, westward
to Heping in Changxi County, and ends at Xiaoshi in Anji County. It is esti-
mated that 60 percent of agricultural and industlial products exported from
AnJi County are transported via this road. The road influence area extends
over 174 kin2, covering 572 villages with a total population of 50,800. The
GOVAI was Y 43.5 million in 1990. The area is rich in mineral reserves and
construction materials. The major agricultural products include food grain
and bamboo. It is estimated that in Anji county alone, the bamboo reserve
accounts for one tenth of the total national reserve.
34.       Existing Road and Traffic Conditions.  The existing road was built
to a low Class 4 standard with gravel surface. The width of the pavement is
only 4.5 m. The daily traffic observed in 1990 was as follows:
Vehicles     100
Tractors     170
Others       300
35.       Proiect Costs and Benefits.  The estimated economic cost of upgrad-
ing this road to a Class 3 standard is Y 3.8 million (Y 3.7 million in terms
of financial cost). Project benefits accrue from savings in vehicle operating
costs. The improved road could be expected to provide better access to the
markets (both inside and outside of the province) for the region and thus
increase agricultural and industrial productivity, in particular the produc-
tion of bamboo and-construction materials. The method used in estimating
project benefits was the same as that used for the Xindeng-Shun'an Road above.



- 85 -ANX I
Page 10
36.       The Economic Rate of Return.  The subproject would yield an NPV of
Y 1 million at a 10 percent discount rate. The EIRR is 14 percent, while the
FYRR is 12 percent.
The Xilanshan-Meilin Road (A-3)
37.       This is a Category A subproject to upgrade an existing gravel road
to a portland cement concrete surface road with better alignment.
38.       The Road Influence Area.  The road is located in the Xiangshan
Peninsula. It extends from Danchen, the capital of Xiangshan County, westward
and ends at Meilin in Ninghai County. The road serves 248 villages with a
total population of 170,000. The major agricultural products include food
grain, cotton, tea, oranges and sugarcane. The area also has a large fishing
industry. The GOVAI in the influence region was Y 680 million in 1990.
39.       The Existing Road and Traffic Conditions.  The existing road was
built to a low Class 4 standard with a gravel macadam surface. tn particular,
the Danchen to Qiangtou section and the section near Hanca both have very low
geometric standards, with the vertical grade reaching 7 percent in some parts.
Daily traffic observed in 1990 was as follows:
Vehicles     370
Tractors     450
Others       500
40.       Prolect Costs and Benefits.  The Danchen to Qiangtou section would
be upgraded according to a Class 3 standard. A tunnel would be built near
Hanca to bypass a section with high vertical grades and to shorten the travel
distance. The estimated economic cost of the subproject is Y 16.0 million
(Y 15.5 million in terms of financial cost). The subproject would reduce the
travel distance from 15.79 km to 14.29 km. The major benefit of the subproj-
ect would be the savings in vehicle operating costs.
41.       The Economic Rate of Return.  The subproject would yield an NPV of
Y 4 million at a 10 percent discount rate, with an EIRR of 14 percent. The
FYRR is 11 percent.
The Xiaoshan-Jinhua (Xiaoshan section) Road (B-4)
42.       This is a Category B subproject to up&rade selected bottleneck sec-
tions of the existing road.
43.       The Road Influence Area.  The road is located in Xiaoshan County,
extending from Xitouwang, via Linpu to Shangfeng. It is an important link
between the rich northern region and the poor, mountainous, southern region.
The road influence area covers 545 km2 with 7,590 villages containing a popu-
lation of 487,100. The land is very fertile and agricultural production of
food grain, silkworm cocoon and tea is abundant. The region also has indus-
tries such as textiles and construction materials and manufactures. Foreign
investment reached $30 million and the GOVAI reached Y 873 million in the area
in 1990.



-86 -                              ANNEX 1
Page I l
44.       The Existina Road and Traffic Conditions.  The existing road was
built to a Class I standard. Many bottlenecks exist along the road. Some of
them are due to low geometric standards, some are due to narrow pavement and
others to poor pavement conditions. These bottlenecks severely impede traffic
flow. In particular, the road passes through the center of several densely
populated towns where mixing traffic imposes severe delays and high traffic
accident rates. The traffic on this section is very heavy. The observed
daily traffic in 1990 was as follows
Vehicles     3,800
Tractors     1,500
Others         500
45.       Proiect Costs and Benefits.  The proposed subproject would upgrade
the road to flat terrain standards. This would include widening, alignment
modification, and pavement rehabilitation in the bottleneck sections. After
this improvement, the road would have a uniform width of 9 meters and the
distance would be shortened from 32.7 km to 29.2 km. The total economic cost
of the improvement is estimated to be Y 42.9 million (Y 41.7 million in terms
of financial cost). The quantifiable projeLt benefits accrue mainly from
savings in vehicle operating costs resulting from the shorter travel distance,
better surface, and reduced congestion. The method used to estimate the bene-
fits from B category subprojects is the same as that used for A category sub-
projects described above.
46.       The Economic Rate of Return.  The NPV of this subproject at 10 per-
cent of the social discount rate is Y 189 million. The subproject has an EIRR
of 34 percent and a FYRR of 11 percent.
Bihu Bridge (C-1)
47.       This is a Category C subproject to replace a ferry with a new
bridge.
48.       The Prolect Influence Area.  The Bihu Bridge (420.82 meters) is
located in the town of Bihu, about 20 km southwest of Lishui City, the capital
of Lishui Prefecture. The Bridge serves Lishui City, Songyang, Yunhe and
Qintian, and the Jin'ling National Autonomous County. The influence area
covers 8,375 km2 containing a population of 1.05 million. The main agricul-
tural production includes food grain, timber and tea. The area is rich in
mineral reserves such as gold and lead. The GOVAI reached Y 93 million in
19900
49.       The Nature of the Proiect and Traffic Conditions.  The Daxi River
runs close to the town of Bihu. There is a road on each bank of the river,
linked by a ferry sta.Lon at Bihu Town, with no bridges crossing the river for
about 20 km up and downstream. The fetry service stops when the water level
is too high in the wet season and too low in the dry season; on average, the
ferry is closed for about one month each year. The Bihu Bridge would be built
to replace the existing ferry station at Bihu township and would provide year-
round access across the river. The observed daily ferry traffic crossing the
river in 1990 was as follows:



- 87 -                             ANNEX I
Page 12
Vehicles         450
Tractors          90
Others           1SO
Passengers     1,933
50.       Proiect Cost and Benefits.  The economic cost of the bridge and
approaches is estimated to be Y 11.9 million (Y 11.6 million in terms of
financial cost). The major quantifiable benefits of the subproject are listed
in the following:
(a) Savinas in Ferry Operating Costs. At present, there are three ferry
boats in operation. The fixed cost, including the capital cost of
the boat, and the variable costs of boat maintenance and ferry oper-
ations are estimated at Y 0.14 million per year. These costs would
be avoided with a bridge.
(b) Savinas in Passenaer Travel Time. At present, three boats operate
at the ferry station. It takes twenty minutes per round trip for
each boat, and therefore the headway at either side of the ferry
station is 6.7 (20/3) minutes. Assuming that the average waiting
time is one half of the headway, it takes on average 13.4 minutes
(-20/2+6.7/2) for each vehicle to cross the river. Once a 400 meter
long bridge is built, the average travel time for vehicles would be
only 0.6 minute (all vehicles, including tractors, are assumed to
travel at 40 kilometers per hour). The travel-time savings is 12.8
(-13.4-0.6) minutes. Assuming the value of time equal to the value
added in production (which is equal to Y 0.7 per hour), each time a
passenger crosses the river the savings from travel time would be
Y 0.15 (-0.7x{12.8/60}).  In 1995, with 3,113 passengers crossing
the river daily during an 11-month period, the savings in passenger
travel time would be Y 0.1S million.
(c) Vehicle Utilization. For vehicles and tractors, the savings in
travel time can be transformed to higher vehicle utilization.
Assuming that the average vehicle purchase price is Y 60,000 per
vehicle and the average life is 12 years, the vehicle epreciation
cost per year is Y 5,000. Since the average utilizati n of vehicles
in China is about 1,500 hours/year, the economic value of a vehicle
per hour is Y 3.33. Hence, the value of improved vehicle utiliza-
tion is Y 0.7 per vehicle. The average purchase price of hand trac-
tors is about one tenth of that of vehicles. The average life of
hand tractors is much shorter than that of a motor vehicle. The
benefit for each tractor resulting from higher utilization was
assumed to be one sixth of what a motor vehicle gains.
(d)  Savinas in Vehicle Ooeratina Costs for Diverted Traffic.  At pres-
ent, 200 vehicles travel from Bihu Town to Lishui City, all taking
the west bank road which has a gravel surface. Once the bridge is
built, the alternative route on the east bank would be 4 km shorter
and would have a paved surface. It is expected that half of the
traffic would be diverted to the east bank road. The savings in
operating costs for the diverted traffic is derived from two fac-



- 88 -mflI
Page 13
tore: the shorter travel dista-..ce, and better surface conditions.
The savings from reduced travel distance vas estimated at Y 4.36
(-4xl.09) per vehicle. The savings from better surface conditions
was estimated at Y 4.76 C-17x(1.09-0.81)] per vehicle. The savings
resulting from shorter distance and better surface for diverted
traffic was estimated to be Y 0.26 million and Y 0.28 million,
respectively.
(e) Benefit From All-Season ODeration. The water level changes signifi-
cantly between wet and dry seasons. As a result, the ferry remains
open on average only about 11 months of the year. When the f-rry is
closed, the traffic detours through Wucun and Taoshan. The detour
adds 37 km to the trip, only 19 km on paved road. The extra operat-
ing costs incurred for detoured motor vehicles and tractors would be
Y 33.9 (-19x0.81+17x1.09) and Y 8.5 (-33.9/4) per vehicle trip,
respectively. Travelers may choose not to travel instead of detour-
ing a long distance; in this evaluation, only one third of the orig-
inal traffic was estimated to detour. The benefit to traffic which
would not detour can be counted as a generated benefit; this was
assumed to equal half the benefits of normal detoured traffic.
51.       The Economic Rate of Return.  The subproject would yield an NPV of
Y 3 million at a discount rate of 10 percent. The EIRR is 14 percent and the
FYRR is 11 percent.
The Liniu Bridge (C-4)
52.       This is a Category C subproject to replace an existing narrow bridge
with a new bridge.
53.       The Existina Conditions.  Linpu bridge is located in Linpu Township
on the Hangzhou-Jinhua Road, and provides a major access from the northern to
the southern region of the province. The existing bridge is only 5.3 m wide
and the approaches have an extremely low geometric design standard. Since the
bridge is located at the center of the town, its approaches also serve as the
city street. As a result, severe delays occur almost every day and the acci-
dent rate is high. The bridge is the most congested bottleneck on the
Hangzhou-Jinhua Road. The observed traffic in 1990 was as follows:
Vehicles     3,800
Tractors     1,500
Others       5,000
54.       Pro1ect Costs and Benefits.  To reduce the traffic congestion and
accidents, a new bridge would be built 940 m downstream to bypass the town.
The bridge is estimated to cost Y 16 million. The major benefit accrues from
savings in vehicle operating costs. Since this bridge subproject is to
replace an inadequate existing bridge, the method used to estimate the sub-
project benefits was the same as that used for Category A subprojects, as
described above.
