Page 14
55.       The Economic Rate of Return.  The subproject is estimated to yield
an NPV of Y 68 million and an EIRR of 35 percent. The FYRR is 11 percent.
The Xiazhai-Jineun Road fD-5)
56.       This is a Category D subproject to replace an existing track with a
new gravel road to bring motorized access to the region.
57.       The Road Influence Area.  The Xiazhai-Jincun road is located on the
border between Yunhe and Songyang County. It extends from Xiazhai Village in
Songyang County, westwu.rd to Chafu, end ends at Jincun Village in Yunhe
County. The area of influence includes 38 vil'uges with a total population of
20,000. Although the Xiazhai-Jineun area is one of the most underdeveloped in
Zhejiang Province, it is rich in natural resources, including timber and min-
eral reserves. The agricultural products in the region include food grain,
tea, fruit and timber.
58.       The Existing Road and Traffic Conditions.  The existing road is
simply a track for human and animal portering; there is no motorable road in
the region at present. All the goods, in or out, are carried by porter or
hand cart. The observed quantities and transport distances of freight flow in
1990 are listed in the following table. It is estimated that the volume will
grow at 5 percent annually until the year 2000. Beyond that, traffic will
grow at 1 percent per year.
Items                           Quantities     Transport Distance      Density
(ton)               (km)            (ton-km)
Mineral Materials                 40,000               3.85            154,000
Fertilizer                           400              8.00               3,200
Food Grain                           625              8.00               5,000
Commercial Agricultural
Products                           200              9.00               1,800
Timber                             1,200              8.00               9,600
Total                        42  425                              173.600
59.       Prolect Costs and Benefits.  The road is classified as a Class 4
mountainous road with a length of 11.7 km. The estimated economic cost of the
road is Y 3.0 million (2.9 million in terms of financial cost). The road
would provide a relatively low-cost access to the region. Its benefits
include both value-added benefits and savings in vehicle operating costs.
Manual transportation will be replaced with vehicle or tractor transportation,
which is much more efficient and results in reduced transport costs. The new
road will also provide a more direct route for diverted traffic. This will
result in additional savings in transport costs. For simplicity, this evalua-
tion only measures the benefit of savings in transport costs, which will lead



- 90-                              AN        I
Page 15
to a conservative estimation of benefits. The quantifiable benefits are cal-
culated as the followings
(a) Assuming that a person can carry a load of 50 kilograms and travel
20 km a day, the volume carried is one ton-km per day at an esti-
mated labor cost of at Y 3.29 per day (according to Provincial
Authorities). Therefore, the transport cost via porterage is Y 3.29
per ton-km. At a load factor of 65 percent, the transport cost
using vehicles is equal to Y 0.50 (1.29/4/0.65) per ton-km, a saving
in transport costs of Y 2.79/ton-km.
(b) The opening up of the region to motorized access would increase
economic activities and freight flow in the area. Assuming that the
generated freight volume is 10 percent of the normal volume and the
benefit accruing from the transport cost savings is half of that of
the normal freight, the value added would be 5 percent of the trans-
port cost savings of normal freight.
(c) It is observed that 2 buses and 8 trucks travel between Dadongba and
Yunhe daily in 1990. The construction of the new road will provide
an alternative route between Wenshuikou to Shuanggang for these
vehicles. The existing route is composed of a 46 km unpaved section
and a 13 km paved section. The alternative route'via the proposed
oubproject would be 37 km, unpaved. The truck operating costs on
the paved and unpaved surfaces are Y 0.76/veh-km and Y 1.29/veh-km,
respectively. The bus operating costs on the paved and unpaved sur-
faces are Y 2.07/veh-km and Y 3.06/veh-km. The per-vehicle savings
on the alternative route would be Y 21.49/veh-km (1.29x46+0.76x13-
1.29x37) for trucks and Y 54.45/veh-km (3.06x46+2.07x13-3.06x37) for
buses.
60.       The construction of the penetration road is expected to lead to an
increase in agricultural production. It also would influence crop composition
by enabling farmers to respond better to market opportunities. In addition,
it would encourage the development of village industry. These benefits were
not quantified in the evaluation. Therefore, the estimated benefits are con-
sidered to be conservative.
61.       In addition, there will be a maintenance cost associated with the
new road. This cost was estimated using the same methodology outlined for the
Zindeng-Shun' an Road.
62.       The subproject will yield an NPV of Y 2 million at a discount rate
of 10 percent per year. The EIRR is 21 percent and the FYRR is 18 percent.

CHINA
ZHEJWIG PROVINCIAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
Road Development Proaram
Procedure for Environmental Analysis of Subpro1ects
Environmental Screening of Subvrojects
1.        Under the RDP, 33 subprojects have been tentatively identified for
implementation in a phased manner over about a four-year period. The objec-
tive of an environmental screening process is to separate the Road Development
Program (RDP) subprojects into one of two categories:
Category I: Subprojects that only include improvement/rehabilitation of
the roads within an ezAsting right-of-way (ROW), minor realignment that
might be needed for localized improvement of traffic "bottlenecks," and
subprojects which are not located within or are not affecting environmen-
tally sensitive areas as broadly defined in footnote 1/ i e , subprol-
ects with no potentially siRnificant environmental impact.
Category II: Subprojects that require substantial widening and acquisi-
tion of new ROW and roads on new alignment. Such subprojects could have
a Potentially adverse imoact on environmentally sensitive areas.
Initial Environmental. Evaluation
2.        Subprojects falling into Category II following the screening exer-
cise would undergo an initial environmental evaluation. If the outcome of the
evaluation confirms that the subproject has no potential adverse impact on the
environment, it would be treated as a Category I subproject.
Actions Reauired - Catesorv I Suboroiects
3.        For Category I subprojects, the Code of Sound Environmental Practice
would be applied during the engineering design and implementation phase. An
outline of possible actions is listed in para. 7 below. This outline would be
expanded in accordance with the particular circumstances of the various sub-
I/  An environmentally sensitive urea would include zones of significant
human habitation (urbanized areas, residential concentrations, hospitals,
schools, commercial and market complexes), ecologically important zones
such as wetlands, forests or any area relatively unaltered by humans
(i.e., an area that is described by PRC National/Provincial environmental
staff as being in a "natural" state), archeological/historicallcultural
sites, and terrain with slope greater than 50 percent--especially when
soils are erodible and near surface waters.



-93                              ANNEX
Apoendix 2,
Page 2
projects.  An Environmental Action Plan would be prepared listing the particu-
lar measures to be taken and the entities responsible for the activities.
Supervision and monitoring activities and responsibilities for each of the
subprojects wc'uld also be identified. The EAP would not need to be elaborate.
It could be supplemented by a aummary table such as the one presented in
Appendix 1 of Annex 2 for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway.
Actions Required - Categorv II Subprolects
4.        Each of the subprojects remaining in Category II would require an
Environmental Assessment and the preparation of a comprehensive EAP. The
scope of these activities would be dictated by the complexity of the environ-
mental risk involved, but in general, the ELe and the EAPs should identify and
provide appropriate measures for dealing withs (a) areas of human congrega-
tion that might be affected by noise and dust caused by road constructions
(b) archeological/cultural/historic sites that might be affected by vibration,
dust and construction support facilities; (c) sensitive ecological areas such
as surface waters, wetlands/swamps, forests, etc. that could be directly
affected by effluent from construction eites and storage facilities, or indi-
rectly by induced land use changes; (d) appropriate sampling methodologies,
frequencies and analytical methods to be used for monitoring potential air,
water, noise, ecological and social/cultural/historical impacts; aid (e) orga-
nizational responsibility for management and monitoring functions.
Organizational Arrangements
S.        The above-outlined activities would be carried out under the overall
directives of ZPTD through ZPHAB and the prefectural/municipal/county trans-
port administration units, with the support of the Zhejiang Provincial Envi-
ronmental Protection Bureau end the Zhejiang Provincial Environmental Protec-
tion Research Institute.
Bank Requirements for Clearance
6.        One of the conditions of acceptance for financing under the project
would be the presentation to the Bank of an RAP for Category I subprojects and
an EA and an RAP for Category II subprojects, satisfactory to the Bank.
An Outline of the Code of Sound Environmental Practice for Subproiects
7.        The following list covers the most likely relevant elements of such
a code. The list should be reviewed and expanded as considered appropriate in
the course of the environmental analysis:
(a)  All temporary and permanent fill/spoil placement sites and exposed
batters are to be protected against erosion by use of mulch, fabric
and/or planting of vegetation as soon as possible (especially during
rainy seasons);
(b)  Materials stores (especially fuel storage and dispensing area.)
should be protected by a ring drain and collection pit to prevent



-94
Anpendix 2
Page 3
accideatal spills from *ntering surface waters. All bitumen,
cement, chemicals and fuels should be properly stored and covered to
prevent spills and mobilization by runoff water. All lubricants
should be collected and recycled (if recycling is possible, other-
wise disposed into an acceptable location);
(c) Periodically water down temporary roads, exposed road surfaces dur-
ing construction, fill/spoil sites until such can be vegetated, and
all other exposed surfaces from which dust could be mobilized in
unacceptably high volume;
(d) Installation of adequate temporary drainage along construction work,
spoil disposal, quarry and borrow sites;
(e) Temporarily store and stabilize all topsoil removed along the ROW,
in preparation for the opening of new borrow and quarry areas, and
other areas, wherever possible (to be used in rehabilitation of
construction sites, quarries, borrow pits, atc);
(f) Rehabilitate, where possible, to previous land use those areas used
for borrow pits, quarries, and spoil disposal. Where this is not
possible, rehabilitate to the most appropriate land use possible,
considering surrounding 'and use;
(g) Provide traffic control at areas where road construction, construc-
tion traffic into the site of construction, construction support
facilities (offices, workshops and stores, maintenance yards, etc.)
and quarries/borrow areas could cause traffic impacts. Post and
control speed of construction-related vehicles along access routes
and within the ROW;
(h) Ensure all construction equipment is fitted with proper equipment
for the control of noise and engine exhaust;
(i) Construction activities in sensitive areas (for example, residential
areas, hospitals, bird nesting and other areas where noise and human
activity may disrupt animal life cycles) should be limited and/or
prohibited during evening and early morning hours;
(j) Prohibit poaching under terms of employment for construction staff;
(k) Adequate temporary pedestrian and vehicle crossings are to be pro-
vided, controlled and maintained during construction;
(1) Spoil from excavation (cut to spoil, tunnels, etc.) in areas with
slope greater than 50 percent should be transported to a suitable
spoil disposal area, not "side-tipped" in steep terrain; and
(m) Spoil disposal sites should have bottom (seepage) and uphill inter-
ceptor drains installed. Spoil should be placed and compacted and
the downslope side stabilized to prevent slips.

CHINA
ZHEJIANG PROVINCIAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
Environmental Assessment and Action Plan Summary
for the Hanazhou-Ninabo Hiahwav
A. Environmental Assessment
Backaround
1.        The policy and administrative requirements for the environmental
assessment of highway projects in China are stipulated in the following docu-
ments:
(a) Management Guidelines on Environmental Protection of Construction
Projects of PRC, 1986;
(b) Regulations for Engineering Design on Environmental Protection of
Construction Projects, 1987;
(c) Directive No. 17 of the Ministry of Communications, PRC/1990, Man-
agement Guidelines on Environmental Protection of Construction Proj-
ects; and
(d) Nanas.ment Procedures for Environmental Protection of Construction
Projects, NEPA, 199A,
2.        The PTDs are required to undertake, directly or through a tGehnical
institution, an Initial Environmental Analysis for submission to the Provin-
cial Environmental Protection Bureaus at the prefeasibility stage of a highway
proposal. For major infrastructure projects, the next step is the carrying
out of an EA. Only specially licensed institutions can undertake eAs in
China. A completed EA would be submitted to HOC for preevaluation, which is
normally done by a panel of experts including representatives of the National
Environmental Protection Agency (DEPA). NEPA's clearance of the EA is
required for projects coating more than Y 200 million and for obtaining SPC's
approval to go ahead with the engineering design for proposed highway proj-
ects.
3.        The above-outlined intornal procedures were followed for the EA
process for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway in a manner interrelated with the Bank
project preparation cycle. An EA for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway was carried
out by ZPEPRI during 1989/90 and was followed by the release of a EA report in
November 1990. The Bank's comments on the report were provided in March 1991,
together with a suggested outline for an EAP. A draft RAP was reviewed during
project appraisal in June 1991, and a final EAP, incorporating the Bank's
comments on the earlier draft, was submitted to the Bank in December 1991,
which provided the basis of the EAP. A summary of this document is presented
In this annex.



-103-                             ANNEX 2
Page 2
Characteristics of the Highwav Corridor
4.        Tovographv and Climate.  The alignment of the Hangzhou-Ningbo High-
way passes through the Xiaoshan, Shaoxing and Ningbo plain, an area of about
7,800 square kilometers. This region of the province is characterized by a
terrain which is higher in the south and west, and lower in the north and east
with a gentle, average grade of 0.02 percent from west to east. The plain is
densely interwoven with a network of rivers and canals. The region lies in a
subtropical monsoon zone and is occasionally hit by typhoons and heavy rain-
storms.
5.        Population.  The area along the alignment of the highway has a popu-
lation of about 6.00 million, dispersed in 14 administrative county units,
averaging 790 individuals per square kilometer. The highway alignment does
not pass any closer than 4 kilometers to towns, but there are abotut 62 vil-
lages that are located within 100 meters of the highway alignment, and seven
villages will be bisected by the route.
6.        Agriculture.  The ROW passes predominantly through irrigated rice
fields. The method of rice farming can be described as intensive cultivation.
Genetically improved varieties seem to be used exclusively, accompanied by
herbicides, pesticides and frequent fertilization. The resulting productivity
of the land is high.
7.        Cultural/Historic.  There are numerous historic sites and tourist
areas in and around the major towns of Hangzhou, Shaoxing, and Ningbo. The
most important of these, and the one closest to the highway ROW, is the Hemudu
site. This is a site of New Stone Age settlement (one of the oldest discov-
ered in China) and is classified as an area of national importance.
8.        Noise.  Existing noise quality in the 67 villages near or around the
highway alignment is generally good. Noise levels are expected to average
48 dY(A) during the day and 43 dB(A) at night. This is based on 24-hour moni-
toring of Yunfeng Village in Pengbu Town. Noise levels in the countryside
were understandably lower, with average noise levels of 42 dB(A) in the day,
and 33-35 dB(A) at night. The existing noise levels measured at the Hemudu
historic site averaged 48 dB(A), although the noise standard for residential
areas was exceeded periodically when boats passed along the Yuyao River, near
the site.
9.        Air Quality.  Ambient air quality at representative sites in the
countryside and villages within 100 meters of the highway ROW was measured.
The baseline monitoring program included analysis of S02, NOX, CO, and TSP
(total suspended particulates). Air quality was similar between village and
country areas with the exception of S02 concentrations, which were higher in
villages because of higher use of coal for heating and in industry. Present
air quality along the ROW is fairly good, except for Sanjiang Village and two
other monitoring points also near roads. The NO, and TSP concentrations were
elevated in these sites because of vehicle emissions and dust being mobilized
off road surfaces.



-l04 -                            ANNEX 2
Page 3
10.      Water Quality.  The water quality of rivers within the study area
was generally suitable for agricultural use. However, rivers and ponds near
the highway ROW at Pengbu and Qianjiang Farm were more polluted, with BOD
(5 day) and COD approaching or exceeding evaluation standards. This is the
result of surface water pollution by solid and human waste.
Potential Environmental Impact Identified in EA
11.       The following key areas of environmental impact were identified,
where adverse changes may take place during the construction and operation of
the new highway:
(a) Social disruption would directly affect an estimated 13,000 individ-
uals who will be resettled or compensated for severance (Annex 3).
Furthermore, the highway will interrupt communication between commu-
nities and will constrain access to some locations inconveniencing
work or mobility that would be related to social activity.
(b) Air pollution could be caused by dust generated during construction,
primarily by earthmoving machinery and trucks and also by stockpiled
materials at depots and quarries. The operation of the asphalt
plants and construction traffic could produce excessive noxious
fumes. At the operation phase, there could also be some localized
concentration of SO2 or NO, during hours of peak traffic and under
certain weather conditions along the highway.
(c) Water 2ollution could occur during construction from excessive spil-
lage of earth and other building materials, such as lime and fly-
ash, into irrigation channels and waterways. During construction
and operation of the highway, there is also a potential for water
contamination from oil and fuel leaks from gas stations and fuel
depots where surface waters run close to such spots.
(d) Noise levels could become excessive from the operation of plants and
machinery, as well as from normal traffic after the opening of the
highway wherever the new highway alignment will be close to vil-
lages. Seven schools have been identified as requiring mitigating
measures against excessive noise levels.
(e) Soil erosion could lead to a potential loss of hundreds of tons of
topsoil per year from exposed slopes of cuts and embankments of the
highway and borrow pits.
B. Environmental Action Plan Summary
12.       The EAP stipulates how the environmental impacts identified in the
HA are going to be dealt with at the design, implementation and operation
phaseo of the highway, and what administrative arrangements have been made for
the execution of the HAP during the three distinct phases of the development
of the highway project.



- 105 -                           ANX 2
Page 4
Measures for Mitizatina Environmental Impacts
13.       Social Disruption.  In locating the alignment for the new highway,
four alternative routes were analyzed from the combined viewpoints of environ-
mental impact, engineering feasibility, and economic viability. The alignment
selected was the most advantageous of the alternatives in these respects.  In
particular, it required less land and had less adverse effect on built-up
areas than the other three alternatives. Thus, the extent of involuntary
resettlement has been minimized.  Because of the rather homogeneous nature of
the topography and ecology of the corridor, no other environmental aspects had
substantive relevance to the alignment selection. The detailed analysis for
route selection is covered in documents available in the Project File
(Annex 5). To minimize the disruption to mobility caused by the severance
from villages, and separation of farmlands from residential areas by the high-
way alignment, the engineering design has made provision for the required
number of underpasses for local traffic and pedestrians. Overall, 234 under-
passes will be built for the 138 km long section of highway, an average of one
underpass for each 600 m section. To benefit from the new highway in terms of
improved transportation services, major population centers in the cor-idor
will be linked to the new highway by six interchanges.
14.       Air Oualitv.  To reduce air pollution at the work sites, stockpiled
materials and unsealed access roads used by construction traffic will be
watered regularly and dust collecting bins will be placed at critical areas
along the work sites. Asphalt plants, to the extent possible, will be located
on the lee side of inhabited areas and at least 1,000 m from residences. Air
quality for fumes will be regularly monitored during the construction and the
operation of the highway.
15.       Control of Noise.  The management of noise impacts during construc-
tion and operation is based on distance and the construction of physical bar-
riers. Existing houses and other structures within 20 m of the ROW will be
demolished and no new buildings will be allowed to be constructed within 50 m
of the ROW. The objective is to limit human congregation, eventually, to
areas at least 70 m from the carriageways of the road. Physical barriers to
noise will include earth berms and fences. Trees will be planted along the
ROW boundaries of the highway, and 4 m wide forest belts will be established
at villages. These forest belts will be increased to a 15 m width along the
section near the Hemudu Antiquities site. At sensitive areas along the high-
way, such as schools, a total of 160 m of sound screens (concrete block fence)
will be constructed.
16.       Water Nanaxement4 To eliminate or minimize the risk of flooding,
the bridges and culverts for the highway have been designed for appropriate
flood frequencies according to national standards and based on local histori-
cal flood data. The design of roadside drainage is aimed at minimizing r.ndom
runoff, and discharge would be appropriately controlled. Groundwater pollu-
tion, resulting from rainwater becoming polluted with heavy metals after per-
colation through fly ash, is a minor concern since chemical analysis of the
fly ash to be used has indicated low levels of heavy metals and other metal
pollutants.



-106f -                           AANNEX 2
Page 5
17.       Other mitixatina measures to be taken during construction concern
the impact of construction traffic, operation of quarries and waste disposal
from work compounds and depots. These are also stipulated in the EAP.
Oraanizational end Aonitorinia Arrantements
18.       Environmental protection measures were formula-.d to be taken during
three distinct phases of the highway development: (i) preparation and design
phase; (ii) construction phase; and (iii) operation phase. The management
responsibilities end organizational arrangements under these phases are dif-
ferent, although ZPEPEC has the primary responsibility for all environmental
protection activity.
19.       During the preparation and deslin phase, ZPEPEC mobilized ZPEPRI to
carry out the EA and ZPTDI to carry out the engineering design of the highway.
The design of the highway includes several modifications that are directed at
controlling end eliminating potential environmental impacts. These include
the adequate provision of clearance at bridges and culverts to eliminate or
limit the risk of flooding, physical sound barriers, a well-designed roadside
drainage system, crossroad access, and troatment of slopes to reduce soil
erosion. ZPTDI is also responsible for ensuring that the conditions of con-
tract and the technical specifications appropriately stipulate the contract-
ors' responsibilities regarding environmental protection measures during con-
struction. All design end technical specifications related to environmental
protection have been taken into account in the completion of the engineering
design and bidding documents presented to the Bank at the end of January 1992.
20.       Durint construction, ZPEPEC will exercise control over environmental
matters primarily through the construction supervision organization. The
three main supervision offices, to be set up in Hangzhou, Shaoxing and Ningbo,
in addition to carrying out routine construction supervision duties, will also
monitor the effectiveness of environmental management recommendations and
ensure that the contractors meot their obligations under the contracts with
regard to environmental protection. In addition, two comprehensive, long-term
environmental monitoring stations will be set up in the vicinity of the high-
way alignment and will be operated through the Shaoshing and Ningbo supervi-
sion offices.
21.      TM  operation of the hijhway after its completion will be the
responsibility of ZPZPEC, which will have a central environmental unit and a
unit in each of the three regional administrative offices in Pengbu, Shanjiang
and Duatang.  Also, the two lont-term monitorina stations to be set up for
the construction stage along the hilghway, would remain as permanent environ-
mental monitoring stations. The monitoring program during operation will
primarily measure concentrations of CO, NO, and TSP relating to vehicle and
traffic noise and vibration movement along the new highway. The monitoring
frequency will be five days per season, and five times per day. Two of the
five monitoring days per season will include 24-hour sampling. The other
sampling will only occur during hours of daylight. The station located near
Ningbo City will also include monitoring of ozone and hydrocarbons during the
winter months.



- 107 -                           ANNE  2
Page 6
22.       During both the construction and the operation phoses, ZPEPB, which
is responsible for supervising end administering environmental protection in
the province, will carry out additional environmental monitoring functions
through its county offices.
23.       A sumary on environmental issues, actions to be taken and adminis-
trative responsibilities for implementation is shown in Appendix 1 to this
nnex. The monitoring data for air, noise end water quality are to be mea-
sured against the standards indicated In Appendix 2.
Cost. Monitorina Equlment. Trainina and Reporting
24.       Most of the cost ossociated with the environmental protection has
been incorporated in the project cost under the civil works contracts. The
estimated cost of major items, including the cost of setting up the monitoring
stations in Shaoxing and Ningbo, is Y 4.93 million ($918,000) as detailed in
Appendix 3 to this annex. The testig equipment and Instrumentation needed at
a cost of Y 753,000 ($140,220) and listed in Appendix 4 to this annex, has
been added to the equipment list to be procured under the project (Table 3.2).
25.       The training of technicians for the monitoring stations and those
involved with environmental issuos on the supervision team would be undertaken
before the start of construction works. Same of the training would be locally
organized, but some training courses covering nvironmental issues would be
conducted as part of the training courses organised by the foreign consultant
to be engaged for assisting In the supervision of construction.
26.       Reporting on environmental issues would be included in the quarterly
progress reports on project lmplmentation, which are to be submitted for the
Bank's review. The regular reports and field data collected from the two
monitoring stations would be made available to Bank supervision missions on
requsst.

ZHBJWI  PRICI   HIGHWAY PRWQC
Resettlement Action Plan Sumrv for the hanazhou-Ninnbo Himhwav
Route Locatlon and ImNact
1.        The highway begins at Pengbu, an eastern suburb of Hangshou City.
From there it proceeds In a southeastorn direction across the Quiantang River,
traverses the northern part of the Xiaoshan-Shaoxing plain, enters into the
Shaoxing-Ningbo plain, passes through the northern part of 8hangyu County and
the southern part of Yuyao City, and ends up at Dazhujia, a southern suburb of
Ningbo City. The land affectod by the routo is prlmarily agricultural,
although the rural economy has been diversifying over the years and an
increasing proportion of the population is now engagod in both agricultural
and industrial activities. Thus, of the 12,940 individuals affected by the
highway, only about 47 percent are engaged exclusively in farming, while about
20 percent have income from agriculture as well as from other activities.
About 5 percent work for enterprilse or institutions, and the remaining
28 percent are economically not active. In terms of Impact on properties,
only about 4 percent of the individuals affected would lose their houses,
72 percent would be affected by land acquisition, 19 percent would be affected
by both land and house acquisition, and 5 percent would be affected only indi-
rectly.
Poliec Basis of Land Acquisition and Resettlemnt
2.        The Land Administration Law of PRC and the Zhejiang Provincial Land
Administration Regulations are the legal basis for land acquisition and reset-
tlement to be undertaken in connection with the construction of highways.
However, to initiate action on land acquisition and resettlement for major
highway projects, the provinces involved are required to obtain SPC's approval
of the feasibility study recoammendations and MOC's approval of the preliminary
engineering design.
CoMDensation Policies
3         Based on the above, for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway the following
compensation provisions have been applied:
(a) Standing crops compensation fee - 1/2 x annual production
value of acquired land
(b) Land compensation - 4 x annual production value of
acquired land
(c)  Resettl. subsidy - 2 x 'nm'l proctton value    totialt ac  Ld land
of a:tlrat Lot acquired land



- 115 -A X 3
Pag. 2
(d)  Compensation fee for standing crop will be paid to individual owners.
(e)  Land compensation fees and resettlement *ubsidios will be paid to
county, township or village governments and used collectively. These
governments will use part of this money to finance enterprises which
are able to absorb the surplus labor. In some case., the compensa-
tion may be spent on land reclamation from the sea to increase the
land area for cultivation and employ the labor. Most of the compen-
sation will be administered at the village level.
(f)  Compensation for personal belongings will be paid according to local
practice.
(g)  Compensation for houses will be on a replacement value basis.  Houses
will be reconstructed in the vicinity of the existing dwelling in
conformity with the local township plan.
(h) Factories and enterprises affected will be relocated in the vicinity
of the locality in line with the zoning plan of the city or county.
Principles of Resettlement
4.        The land acquisition involves about 60 townships, but the land to be
acquired as a proportion of the total cultivated land in each township area is
relatively low, on the average about 1.8 percent of the total. The following
four measures will be taken to resettle the affected peoplet
(a) readjusting the land to allow affected people to keep their own
agricultural land;
(b) providing land compensation from reclaimed land (reclaiming from
tidal basins or realigned river beds);
(c)  offering employment opportunities through diversification of agri-
culture-related activity (such as animal husbandry) and township
-enterprises; and
(d) recruiting resettlers for state-run enterprises in nearby cities and
counties.
Resettlement Procedures
5.        Investiaation of Comoensation Standards.  ZPEPEC, the responsible
provincial authority for the resettlement activities for the highway, carried
out an investigation to determine the compensation parameters used in various
*counties along the route during mid-1990. It obtained information including
types of crops, cropping acreage, yields per mu, crop prices, and building
costs. This information was used to calculate the annual production value,
and the land compensation and building unit prices for the land and property
to be acquired. The following unit prices have been used for estimating ROW
and resettlement costs:



- 116 -                            ANNEX 3
Page 3
Item                 Unit      Unit Price (Yuan)
Land Compensation              mu              8,000
Borrowed Land Compensation    mu               3,000
Buildings                      m2                150
Power Poles                    each            2,500
Telecommunication Poles        each            2,000
Pumping Stations               each               390
Brick - Kiln                   each               600
Tombs                          each               150
6.        Oraanizational Arrangements.  The organization for land acquisition
and resettlement was established during April-September 1991 as follows:
(a) The provincial, city, county and township governments are responsi-
ble for decision-makina on their respective levels to put into
effect the land acquisition and resettlement policies and proce-
dures.
(b) Leading groups have been formed at provincial, city and county lev-
els by tho heads of planning, land administration, urban construc-
ti'on, communications, water conservation, electric power, post, and
broadcasting departments responsible for coordinatina land acquisi-
tion and resettlement activities in their respective sectors.
(c) The land acquisition and resettlement division of ZPEPEC at the
provincial level, and the land acquisition and resettlement depart-
ments at the city and county levels are responsible for the execu-
tion of land acquisition and resettlement. About 70 personnel are
involved at these levels in the process. In addition, all 62
affected townships along the route have set up land acquisition and
resettlement agencies, staffed by 5 to 7 persons respectively and
totaling about 330, who are responsible for land acquisition and
resettlement at the township level. Furthermore, every village
within a township has specific staff assigned to be responsible for
land acquisition and resettlement, about 5 persons for every village
and about 1,600 persons for the whole alignment involved at the
village level.
7.        Survey of the ROW.  The stakeout of the highway's ROW in the field
was carried out during September-October 1991 by ZPTDI. The land authorities
at various levels checked the ROW with the county and township resettlement
offices and delineated township and village boundaries on drawings. The
county resettlement office is responsible for checking the quantity of
acquired land and other properties within the ROW with the relative township
resettlement office. The records are prepared in a unified format and are
submitted to the county resettlement offices for summary. The summary then
provides the basis for the land acquisition and resettlement agreements. The
quantity survey was completed at the end of November 1991.
8.        Contracts.  The agreements authorizing to proceed with the land
acquisition and resettlement were signed between ZPEPEC and the counties



-117 -                             ANNEX 3
Page 4
involved in mid-December 1991, and the contracts with the respective townships
and villages for the execution of the actions involved have been signed subse-
quently. All authorization procedures were completed by mid-January 1992.
9.        Comiletion of ROW Acquisition and Resettlement.  The demolition
works within the ROW would be carried out between mid-January and the end of
March 1992, and the redistribution of land would also be completed during the
saLd time period. Final checking and site inspection by ZPEPEC is scheduled
for April, 1992. The building of houses for the resettlers will be organized
by the owners, and both the resettlement and the transfer of the highway's ROW
are scheduled to be completed by the end of May 1992.
Monitorina
10.       The implementation of the RAP for the Hangzhou-Ningbo Highway would
be monitored through quarterly progress reporting by the ZPEPEC, as executive
agency, and routine Bank supervision missions. The monitoring of the effec-
tiveness and the socioeconomic impact of resettlement on the population
affected would be undertaken after a certain time interval following the
resettlement actions.  The scope and methodology for such monitoring would be
agreed between the Bank and Zhejiang Province based on a plan to be submitted
to the Bank by June 30, 1993.
Summaries
11.       A set of data regarding acquisition and resettlement, and covering
the population affected, land and property being required, and cost involved
is presented in Appendix 1 and Appendix 2 to this annex.
